Refining the Definition of Khabar
Author refines the definition of khabar to address the objection that some statements are necessarily true or false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the definition of khabar as 'admitting truth and falsehood' applies to the essence, not to every individual instance.

Core Idea
Khabar's definition is about its nature, not each case.

The standard definition of khabar (informative statement) as 'that which admits truth and falsehood' seems to exclude necessarily true or false statements. In this passage, the commentator resolves the objection by distinguishing the essence of khabar from its individual instances. Understanding this refinement is crucial for correctly applying logical analysis to statements in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The common definition of khabar is that it admits truth and falsehood. The objection is that statements like 'Allah is our God' or 'The earth is above us' only admit one of the two, so they would be excluded. Note the terms 'āmm (general) and its failure.
The objection challenges the comprehensiveness of the definition.
2
Evaluate the weak response
The commentator mentions a weak response that interprets the waw (و) in 'admitting truth and falsehood' as meaning 'or' (أو). He dismisses it because it does not solve the logical issue.
Recognize that changing conjunction does not address the root problem.
3
Understand the proper resolution
The correct solution is to interpret 'admitting truth and falsehood' as referring to the essence (mahiyya) of khabar, not to every individual. The essence of khabar is to be a statement that can be true in some instances and false in others, even if a particular instance is necessarily true or false due to its content.
This distinction preserves the definition's universality.
4
Apply the resolution to an example
Take 'Allah is our God'. This statement is always true, but the type of statement (a compound attribution) can be false in other cases, like 'The idol is our god'. Thus the essence of khabar remains unchanged.
Focus on the type, not the token.
5
Summarize the key insight
The passage concludes that each individual khabar is actually either true or false, but the type itself admits both. This is the refined understanding that should be memorized.
This insight prevents confusion in logical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شأنه عرف الخبر أولاً بما يحتمل الصدق والكذب كما هو المشهور، وفسره بوجه يندفع منه الإشكال الوارد عليه، وتفصيله: أن هذا التعريف غير جامع؛ لأنه يخرج منه الأخبار التي تحتمل الصدق فقط دون الكذب، كقولنا: الله إلهنا ومحمد رسول الله أو بالعكس، كقولنا : الأرض فوقنا والسماء تحتنا؛ فإنها لا تحتمل الصدق والكذب، والجواب بأن الواو الواصلة ههنا بمعنى أو الفاصلة ليس بشيء؛ فإنه يستدرك حينئذ لفظ يحتمل، كما لا يخفى وجه الدفع بما فسره الشارح ظاهر؛ فإن المراد من احتمال الصدق والكذب أن يكون من شأن الخبر الاتصاف بهما بأن يتصف في بعض الأفراد بالصدق وفي بعضها بالكذب، فماهية الخبر من حيث ذاتها تحتمل الصدق والكذب؛ فإن ماهية الخبر هو مفهوم قولنا هذا ذاك وإن كان بعض الأفراد ممتنع الاتصاف بأحدهما بالنظر إلى خصوصية المادة. هكذا حقق السيد السند الشريف قدس سره. له: أي للمركب التام الخبري صادق أو كاذب إشارة إلى أن نوع الخبر وإن اتصف بالصدق والكذب لكن كل فرد من أفراد يكون في نفس الأمر إما متصفا بالصدق فقط أو بالكذب. فافهم واحفظ.
His status: He first defined the informative statement as that which admits truth and falsehood, as is well-known, and explained it in a way that repels the objection raised against it. Its detail: This definition is not comprehensive because it excludes those informative statements that admit only truth without falsehood, like our saying: 'Allah is our God and Muhammad is His Messenger', or the opposite, like our saying: 'The earth is above us and the sky is below us'; for they do not admit truth and falsehood. The answer that the conjunctive waw here is in the sense of the disjunctive 'or' is nothing; then the word 'admits' would be added, as is not hidden. The repelling is by what the commentator explained: that what is meant by 'admitting truth and falsehood' is that it is in the nature of the informative statement to be characterized by them, in that it is characterized by truth in some individuals and by falsehood in others. So the essence of the informative statement in itself admits truth and falsehood; for the essence of the informative statement is the concept of our saying 'this is that', even if some individuals are incapable of being characterized by one of them due to the particularity of the material. This is how al-Sayyid al-Sanad al-Sharif, may God sanctify his secret, verified it. For it: i.e., for the perfect informative compound, it has truth or falsehood, indicating that the type of informative statement, although characterized by truth and falsehood, each individual is in reality either characterized by truth alone or by falsehood. So understand and memorize.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage deals with the refinement of the definition of khabar (informative statement) in logic. The commentator begins by noting that the common definition of khabar as 'that which admits truth and falsehood' has an issue: it seems to exclude statements that are necessarily true, like 'Allah is our God', or necessarily false, like 'The earth is above us'. Such statements do not admit both truth and falsehood; they are fixed. The objection is that the definition is not comprehensive. The commentator dismisses a weak response that the 'waw' in 'admitting truth and falsehood' means 'or' (i.e., admits truth or falsehood). Instead, he explains the proper resolution: the definition should be understood as referring to the essence (mahiyya) of khabar, not to every individual instance. The nature of khabar is such that it could be true or false in different individuals; some individuals may be necessarily true or false due to their specific material content, but the essential nature of the statement remains. He attributes this insight to al-Sayyid al-Sanad al-Sharif (possibly al-Sharif al-Jurjani). Then he adds that each individual khabar is actually either true or false, but the type itself admits both. The passage concludes with an exhortation to understand and memorize this point.

Key Terms
الخبر (al-khabar): An informative compound (murakkab tamm khabari) that in its essence admits both truth and falsehood, though individual instances may be fixed. [Note: In later logic, khabar is sometimes distinguished from insha' (performative) in terms of truth-aptness, but the core definition remains stable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is the definition of khabar as 'admitting truth and falsehood' to be properly understood?
A) Every individual statement must be both true and false.
B) The essence of khabar is such that it can be true in some instances and false in others.
C) The statement must contain the word 'maybe'.
D) The listener must be uncertain.

Key Takeaway
The definition of khabar applies to its essence, not to every individual instance; this preserves comprehensiveness despite necessarily true or false statements.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement 'Allah is eternal'. Is it a khabar according to the refined definition? Explain why, using the distinction between essence and individual instance.
?
Identify a statement from the Quran that is necessarily true (e.g., 'There is no god but Allah'). Show how the refined definition of khabar applies to it.

Classification of Categorical Propositions by Subject
Author categorizes propositions into natural, quantified, and indefinite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish natural, quantified, and indefinite categorical propositions and identify the basis for each classification.

Core Idea
The subject's quantity determines whether a proposition is natural, quantified, or indefinite.

In this passage, the author presents a threefold classification of categorical propositions based on how the subject is considered: the judgment may be on the nature itself (natural), on individuals with a quantifier (quantified), or on individuals without specifying quantity (indefinite). This distinction is foundational for understanding how propositions function in logical reasoning within Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Locate the three types mentioned: natural (ṭabī‘iyya), quantified (maḥṣūra), and indefinite (muhmala). Note that the author lists them in order: second is natural, third quantified, fourth indefinite—implying a first type which is not further defined here.
The first type may be 'partial' (juz’iyya) but is not elaborated.
2
Define the basis of each type
Natural: judgment on the very nature of the subject, not its individuals. Quantified: judgment on individuals, with an explicit quantifier. Indefinite: judgment on individuals, but the quantity is left unspecified.
3
Recall the earlier verifiers’ refinement
The earlier verifiers (al-mutaqaddimūn al-muḥaqqiqūn) stated that the subject in all propositions is a mental entity (al-amr al-dhihnī). The difference lies in the consideration: natural considers it without extension to individuals; quantified with extension; indefinite as it is without condition.
This metaphysical subtlety affects how propositions are interpreted in theological debates.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Take the proposition 'Human is a rational animal'. If understood as a judgment on the nature of humanity, it is natural. If understood as 'Every human is a rational animal', it is quantified (universal affirmative). If understood without quantity, it is indefinite.
The same wording can belong to different types depending on intent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التصديقات
أقسام الحملية باعتبار الموضوع
أو جزئية، وما به البيان سور، وإلا فمهملة
والثاني طبعية، والثالث محصورة والرابع مهملة. ثم المحصورة إن بين فيها أن الحكم على كل أفراد الموضوع فكلية، وإن بين أن الحكم على بعض أفراده فجزئية، وكل منهما إما موجبة أو سالبة. ولابد في كل من تلك المحصورات الأربع من أمر يبين كمية أفراد الموضوع يسمى ذلك الأمر بالسور، أخذ من سور البلد؛ إذ كما أن سور البلد محيط به، كذلك هذا الأمر محيط بما حكم عليه من أفراد الموضوع. فسور الموجبة الكلية هو "كل" ولام الاستغراق وما يفيد معناهما من أي لغة كانت وسور الموجبة الجزئية هو "بعض" و "واحد" وما يفيد مفادهما، وسور السالبة الكلية "لا شيء" و"لا واحد ونظائرهما، وسور السالبة الجزئية "ليس كل" و ليس بعض" و "بعض ليس" وما يساويها.
طبعية لأن الحكم فيها على نفس طبيعة الموضوع دون أفراده. مهملة : لأن بيان كمية أفراد موضوعها مهمل ومتروك فيها. واعلم أن كلامه على ما اختاره من أن الحكم في الطبعية على نفس طبيعة الموضوع وفي المحصورة والمهملة على أفراده والمتقدمون المحققون لما رأوا أن المحكوم عليه في الحقيقة ليس الأمر الحاصل في النفس قالوا: إن الموضوع في كل من الطبيعية والمحصورة هو الأمر الذهني، أي نفس الحقيقة، والفرق أن نفس الحقيقه المأخوذة من حيث أن يحكم عليها بما لا يتعدى إلى أفرادها موضوع الطبعية، ومن حيث أن يحكم عليها بما يتعدى إلى أفرادها موضوع المحصورة ومن حيث أنها أخذت هي هي بلا زيادة شرط موضوع المهملة القدمائية (عبد)
مهملة لإهمال كمية أفراد الموضوع. إسماعيل)
الأربع أي الموجبة الكلية والجزئية والسالبة الكلية والجزئية.(إسماعيل)
من حيث
من أمر إلخ هذا الأمر أعم من أن يكون لفظا كلفظة كل أو بعض وغيرهما أو لا كوقوع النكرة تحت النفي؛ فإنه سور للسلب الكلي مع أنه ليس بلفظ (إسماعيل) سور البلد يعني أن تسمية ذلك الأمر سورا مأخوذ من سور البلد، أي ما يحيطها باعتبار أن هذا الأمر محيط لجميع أفراد الموضوع أو لبعضه كما أن سور البلد محيط به.(إسماعيل)
[The topic of] assents. Sections of categorical propositions with respect to the subject. Or partial, and that which clarifies [the quantity] is a quantifier, otherwise [it is] indefinite. The second is natural, the third is quantified, and the fourth is indefinite. Then the quantified: if it is clarified therein that the judgment applies to every individual of the subject, it is universal; and if it is clarified that the judgment applies to some of its individuals, it is partial. Each of them is either affirmative or negative. In each of those four quantified [propositions], something is required that clarifies the quantity of the individuals of the subject; that thing is called the quantifier (sawr), derived from the wall of a city; for just as the wall of a city surrounds it, so this thing surrounds what is judged upon of the individuals of the subject. So the quantifier of the universal affirmative is 'every' (kull) and the lām of generality and whatever conveys their meaning in any language. The quantifier of the partial affirmative is 'some' (ba‘ḍ) and 'one' (wāḥid) and whatever conveys their meaning. The quantifier of the universal negative is 'nothing' (lā shay’) and 'not one' (lā wāḥid) and their equivalents. The quantifier of the partial negative is 'not every' (laysa kull) and 'not some' (laysa ba‘ḍ) and 'some not' (ba‘ḍ laysa) and what equals them. Natural, because the judgment therein is upon the very nature of the subject without its individuals. Indefinite, because the clarification of the quantity of the individuals of its subject is neglected and left out. And know that his statement, according to his choice, is that the judgment in the natural [proposition] is upon the very nature of the subject, and in the quantified and indefinite [propositions] it is upon its individuals. The earlier verifiers, when they saw that what is judged upon in reality is not the mental content, said: The subject in both the natural and the quantified is the mental thing, i.e., the very reality; the difference is that the very reality taken in respect of being judged upon with something that does not extend to its individuals is the subject of the natural [proposition]; and in respect of being judged upon with something that extends to its individuals is the subject of the quantified; and in respect of being taken as it is without any additional condition is the subject of the indefinite of the ancients. (ʻAbd) Indefinite due to neglecting the quantity of the individuals of the subject. (Ismaʻil) The four: i.e., universal affirmative, partial affirmative, universal negative, and partial negative. (Ismaʻil) 'From' (explanation of 'something' etc.): This 'thing' is more general than being a word such as 'every' or 'some' or others, or not [a word], such as the occurrence of an indefinite noun under negation; for it is a quantifier for universal negation even though it is not a word. (Ismaʻil) 'Wall of the city': means that the naming of that thing as a quantifier is derived from the wall of the city, i.e., what surrounds it, in consideration that this thing surrounds all individuals of the subject or some of them, just as the wall of the city surrounds it. (Ismaʻil)
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of the classification of categorical propositions (al-ḥamliyya) based on their subject. The author begins by listing four types: natural (ṭabī‘iyya), quantified (maḥṣūra), indefinite (muhmala), and another type? Actually the text says 'the second is natural, the third is quantified, and the fourth is indefinite'. The first type is not explicitly named here but from context it appears to be the 'partial' (juz’iyya) mentioned earlier? The phrasing 'or partial' suggests an alternative naming. The author then focuses on the quantified proposition (maḥṣūra), which is further divided into universal (kulliyya) and partial (juz’iyya), each either affirmative or negative. These are the four standard quantified propositions: universal affirmative, universal negative, partial affirmative, partial negative. The text then explains that every quantified proposition requires a quantifier (sawr) that indicates the quantity of the subject, metaphorically derived from the wall (sūr) of a city that surrounds it. The quantifiers are listed: 'kull' for universal affirmative, 'ba‘ḍ' for partial affirmative, 'lā shay’' for universal negative, and 'laysa kull' etc. for partial negative. The author then clarifies the natural proposition: the judgment is upon the nature itself (e.g., 'Human is an animal' meaning the species human), not its individuals. The indefinite proposition leaves the quantity unspecified. A further note from the 'earlier verifiers' (al-mutaqaddimūn al-muḥaqqiqūn) explains that the subject in all propositions is a mental entity (al-amr al-dhihnī), i.e., the reality itself; the difference lies in how that reality is considered: in natural propositions, it is considered with judgment that does not extend to individuals; in quantified, it extends; in indefinite, it is taken without any condition. Marginal comments add that the quantifier does not have to be a word; an indefinite noun under negation can serve as a quantifier for universal negation. The analogy of the city wall is elaborated: the quantifier surrounds the individuals of the subject just as a wall surrounds a city. Overall, this passage provides a clear classification of categorical propositions with definitions of quantifiers and their functions.

Key Terms
الحملية (al-ḥamliyya): A categorical proposition, i.e., a proposition that predicates something of a subject (e.g., 'Zayd is standing'). [Note: Stable across the tradition; synonymous with qaḍiyya ḥamliyya.]
طبعية (ṭabī‘iyya): A natural proposition: one in which the judgment applies to the very nature of the subject, not to its individuals (e.g., 'Human is an animal' meaning the species). [Note: Some logicians treat natural propositions as a distinct type; others reduce them to universal propositions. This text maintains the classical distinction.]
محصورة (maḥṣūra): A quantified proposition: one in which the quantity (universal or partial) is explicitly stated via a quantifier. [Note: Standard term in Aristotelian logic; remains consistent.]
مهملة (muhmala): An indefinite proposition: one where the quantity of the subject is not specified (e.g., 'Human is an animal' in the sense of 'some humans' or 'all humans'?). It leaves the quantity neglected. [Note: In some later logic texts, indefinite propositions are considered equivalent to particular propositions; here it is a distinct type.]
سور (sawr): Quantifier: a linguistic tool that indicates the quantity of the subject in a quantified proposition, e.g., 'kull', 'ba‘ḍ', 'lā shay’'. [Note: Derived from the Arabic word for 'wall'; used consistently in logic.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what characterizes a natural proposition (al-ṭabī‘iyya)?
A) It contains an explicit quantifier like 'kull'.
B) The judgment applies to the very nature of the subject, not its individuals.
C) It is always universal affirmative.
D) It has no subject.

Key Takeaway
Categorical propositions are classified by subject into natural (judging the essence), quantified (with explicit quantifier), and indefinite (no quantity specified).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following proposition as natural, quantified, or indefinite: 'Animal is a body' (al-ḥayawānu jismun). Explain your reasoning based on the author's criteria.
?
Take the proposition 'Some jurists are knowledgeable' (ba‘ḍu l-fuqahā’i ‘ulamā’u). Which type is it, and why is it not natural or indefinite?

Quantifiers (Sawr) for the Four Quantified Propositions
Author explains quantifiers for the four quantified propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to match each of the four quantified propositions with its correct quantifier (sawr).

Core Idea
Each quantified proposition has a specific quantifier that acts like a wall enclosing the subject's individuals.

After classifying propositions, the author focuses on quantified propositions (maḥṣūra) and explains the quantifiers that indicate their quantity. The word 'sawr' is derived from city walls because the quantifier surrounds the individuals of the subject. This lesson identifies the quantifiers for universal affirmative, universal negative, partial affirmative, and partial negative.

Guided Path
1
List the four quantified propositions
They are: universal affirmative (mūjaba kulliyya), universal negative (sāliba kulliyya), partial affirmative (mūjaba juz’iyya), and partial negative (sāliba juz’iyya). The author calls them 'the four' (al-arba‘).
2
Identify the quantifier for each
UA: 'kull' (كل), 'lām istighrāq', or equivalents. PA: 'ba‘ḍ' (بعض), 'wāḥid' (واحد), or equivalents. UN: 'lā shay’' (لا شيء), 'lā wāḥid' (لا واحد), or equivalents. PN: 'laysa kull' (ليس كل), 'laysa ba‘ḍ' (ليس بعض), 'ba‘ḍ laysa' (بعض ليس), or equivalents.
The quantifier can be a word or a contextual element, like an indefinite noun under negation for UN.
3
Understand the metaphor of the wall
The term 'sawr' is borrowed from the wall (sūr) of a city. Just as a wall surrounds the city, the quantifier surrounds all or some individuals of the subject, delimiting the scope of judgment.
4
Apply to Islamic legal propositions
For example, 'Kullu muskirin ḥarāmun' (Every intoxicant is forbidden) is UA with quantifier 'kull'. 'Lā shay’a mina l-muskirāti ḥalālun' (No intoxicant is permissible) is UN with 'lā shay’'. Such propositions are the building blocks of legal reasoning.

Source Text

الأربع أي الموجبة الكلية والجزئية والسالبة الكلية والجزئية.(إسماعيل)

[The topic of] assents. Sections of categorical propositions with respect to the subject. Or partial, and that which clarifies [the quantity] is a quantifier, otherwise [it is] indefinite. The second is natural, the third is quantified, and the fourth is indefinite. Then the quantified: if it is clarified therein that the judgment applies to every individual of the subject, it is universal; and if it is clarified that the judgment applies to some of its individuals, it is partial. Each of them is either affirmative or negative. In each of those four quantified [propositions], something is required that clarifies the quantity of the individuals of the subject; that thing is called the quantifier (sawr), derived from the wall of a city; for just as the wall of a city surrounds it, so this thing surrounds what is judged upon of the individuals of the subject. So the quantifier of the universal affirmative is 'every' (kull) and the lām of generality and whatever conveys their meaning in any language. The quantifier of the partial affirmative is 'some' (ba‘ḍ) and 'one' (wāḥid) and whatever conveys their meaning. The quantifier of the universal negative is 'nothing' (lā shay’) and 'not one' (lā wāḥid) and their equivalents. The quantifier of the partial negative is 'not every' (laysa kull) and 'not some' (laysa ba‘ḍ) and 'some not' (ba‘ḍ laysa) and what equals them. Natural, because the judgment therein is upon the very nature of the subject without its individuals. Indefinite, because the clarification of the quantity of the individuals of its subject is neglected and left out. And know that his statement, according to his choice, is that the judgment in the natural [proposition] is upon the very nature of the subject, and in the quantified and indefinite [propositions] it is upon its individuals. The earlier verifiers, when they saw that what is judged upon in reality is not the mental content, said: The subject in both the natural and the quantified is the mental thing, i.e., the very reality; the difference is that the very reality taken in respect of being judged upon with something that does not extend to its individuals is the subject of the natural [proposition]; and in respect of being judged upon with something that extends to its individuals is the subject of the quantified; and in respect of being taken as it is without any additional condition is the subject of the indefinite of the ancients. (ʻAbd) Indefinite due to neglecting the quantity of the individuals of the subject. (Ismaʻil) The four: i.e., universal affirmative, partial affirmative, universal negative, and partial negative. (Ismaʻil) 'From' (explanation of 'something' etc.): This 'thing' is more general than being a word such as 'every' or 'some' or others, or not [a word], such as the occurrence of an indefinite noun under negation; for it is a quantifier for universal negation even though it is not a word. (Ismaʻil) 'Wall of the city': means that the naming of that thing as a quantifier is derived from the wall of the city, i.e., what surrounds it, in consideration that this thing surrounds all individuals of the subject or some of them, just as the wall of the city surrounds it. (Ismaʻil)
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of the classification of categorical propositions (al-ḥamliyya) based on their subject. The author begins by listing four types: natural (ṭabī‘iyya), quantified (maḥṣūra), indefinite (muhmala), and another type? Actually the text says 'the second is natural, the third is quantified, and the fourth is indefinite'. The first type is not explicitly named here but from context it appears to be the 'partial' (juz’iyya) mentioned earlier? The phrasing 'or partial' suggests an alternative naming. The author then focuses on the quantified proposition (maḥṣūra), which is further divided into universal (kulliyya) and partial (juz’iyya), each either affirmative or negative. These are the four standard quantified propositions: universal affirmative, universal negative, partial affirmative, partial negative. The text then explains that every quantified proposition requires a quantifier (sawr) that indicates the quantity of the subject, metaphorically derived from the wall (sūr) of a city that surrounds it. The quantifiers are listed: 'kull' for universal affirmative, 'ba‘ḍ' for partial affirmative, 'lā shay’' for universal negative, and 'laysa kull' etc. for partial negative. The author then clarifies the natural proposition: the judgment is upon the nature itself (e.g., 'Human is an animal' meaning the species human), not its individuals. The indefinite proposition leaves the quantity unspecified. A further note from the 'earlier verifiers' (al-mutaqaddimūn al-muḥaqqiqūn) explains that the subject in all propositions is a mental entity (al-amr al-dhihnī), i.e., the reality itself; the difference lies in how that reality is considered: in natural propositions, it is considered with judgment that does not extend to individuals; in quantified, it extends; in indefinite, it is taken without any condition. Marginal comments add that the quantifier does not have to be a word; an indefinite noun under negation can serve as a quantifier for universal negation. The analogy of the city wall is elaborated: the quantifier surrounds the individuals of the subject just as a wall surrounds a city. Overall, this passage provides a clear classification of categorical propositions with definitions of quantifiers and their functions.

Key Terms
سور (sawr): Quantifier: a linguistic tool that indicates the quantity of the subject in a quantified proposition, e.g., 'kull', 'ba‘ḍ', 'lā shay’'. [Note: Derived from the Arabic word for 'wall'; used consistently in logic.]
كل (kull): Quantifier for universal affirmative: 'every', 'all'.
بعض (ba‘ḍ): Quantifier for partial affirmative: 'some', 'a certain'.
لا شيء (lā shay’): Quantifier for universal negative: 'nothing', 'not any'.
ليس كل (laysa kull): Quantifier for partial negative: 'not every', 'some not'.

Why the Division of Conditionals is Inductive
Author argues that dividing conditionals is inductive, not necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a logically necessary division and an inductive classification, and identify the empirical basis for the conjunctive/disjunctive split in conditionals.

Core Idea
Some logical divisions are based on survey, not necessity — including conditionals.

In this passage, the author explicitly states that the division of conditional propositions into conjunctive (if...then) and disjunctive (either...or) is not a necessary logical division. Instead, it is based on induction: after surveying all conditional propositions found in the common sciences, only these two types appear. The author is careful to note that the intellect could conceive of other types, but in practice they are not used.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about the division
The author says the division is inductive, not necessary. Understand what that means: it comes from empirical observation, not pure reason.
2
Examine the proof given
The proof states that the relation between the two parts of a conditional is not predicative, so it could be anything; confining it to just two types requires empirical evidence.
The proof is brief but shows why the division cannot be a priori.
3
Understand the empirical basis
The author says: 'when we survey the conditionals, we found only the conjunctive and the disjunctive'. This is an empirical claim based on investigating the sciences.
The term 'tatabbu‘' (perusal) indicates a methodical survey.
4
Evaluate the implication
If the division is inductive, it is not exhaustive in theory, only in practice. The author acknowledges that the intellect could conceive of other conditionals.
This shows epistemic humility: logic acknowledges its limits.

Source Text

And the disjunctive [division] is inductive: i.e., the enumeration that appears after investigation and perusal, even if the intellect allows the opposite due to the lack of limitation between negation and affirmation. For when we survey the conditionals, we found only the conjunctive and the disjunctive, but the intellect may suppose a conditional that is neither conjunctive nor disjunctive, i.e., the judgment in it is not by conjunction nor by disjunction but by something else. (Proof) It is inductive because the conditional has two propositions as its terms in proximate potency, and the relation between the two propositions cannot be that one is predicated of the other, rather there must be a relation other than predication; it is not necessary that the relation other than predication be confined to conjunction and disjunction, for it is possible that it be in another way. So this division is inductive, since in the well-known sciences there is no other relation considered among the terms of propositions.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage addresses the classification of conditional propositions (شرطيات) into conjunctive (متصلة) and disjunctive (منفصلة). The author states that this division is not necessary but inductive (استقرائي). He explains 'استقرائي' as an enumeration that becomes apparent after investigation and survey, even though the intellect could conceive of a conditional that is neither conjunctive nor disjunctive—one where the judgment is based on something else entirely. He then gives a proof: the two parts of a conditional are propositions in proximate potency (not actual judgments), and the relationship between them is not predicative; therefore, it could be anything. Thus, limiting it to only conjunction and disjunction is based on what is actually found in common scientific practice, not on logical necessity. The author is cautious: he acknowledges that other types are possible in theory but are not used. This is a typical Ash'ari or post-classical logical observation emphasizing induction over a priori reasoning. All examples are embedded in the discussion of conditionals, which are central to Islamic logic, e.g., in arguments about 'if...then' in fiqh and kalam.

Conditional Propositions: Connected vs. Disconnected
Author defines conditional propositions and distinguishes muttasila from munfasila, with an objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between connected and disconnected conditional propositions and explain why their definitions remain distinct despite apparent overlap, using the explicit/implicit judgment criterion.

Core Idea
Conditional propositions are defined by connection or repugnancy, but the key is whether the judgment is explicit or implicit.

In this passage, the author defines conditional propositions and splits them into connected (muttasila) and disconnected (munfasila), each with positive and negative forms. An important objection arises: the definitions seem to apply to each other's examples. The answer reveals a crucial logical subtlety: the distinction between explicit (sarih) and implicit (iltizami) judgment. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly classifying conditional propositions in Islamic logic and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining feature of a conditional proposition
A conditional proposition is one where the judgment is not a simple affirmation or denial of something of something, but depends on a condition (supposition of another relation). Look for the phrase 'upon the supposition of another relation' in the text.
The author lists four possibilities: affirming a relation under a condition, negating that affirmation, judging repugnancy between relations, or negating that repugnancy.
2
Extract the four types from the definition
From the four possibilities, two main categories emerge: muttasila (connected) where connection is judged, and munfasila (disconnected) where repugnancy is judged. Each has a positive and negative form. Write them down: positive muttasila, negative muttasila, positive munfasila, negative munfasila.
Examples from the passage: 'If the sun is rising, then night exists' (positive muttasila); 'It is not that if the sun is rising, then night exists' (negative muttasila); 'The number is either even or odd' (positive munfasila); 'It is not that the number is either even or divisible into two equal parts' (negative munfasila).
3
Understand the objection about overlapping definitions
The objector claims that the definition of a negative muttasila (negation of a relation under a condition) applies to a positive munfasila, because in 'The number is either even or odd' the implicit meaning is 'It is not that the number is even and odd' – which is a negation of a relation under a condition. Conversely, the definition of positive munfasila seems to apply to negative muttasila because 'It is not that if the sun is rising, night exists' implies repugnancy between sunrise and night.
This objection threatens to collapse the distinction between the two types.
4
Grasp the answer: explicit vs. implicit judgment
The answer distinguishes explicit (sarih) and implicit (iltizami) judgment. In a disconnected proposition (munfasila), the judgment of repugnancy is explicit, while the negation of connection is only implicit (necessary implication). In a connected proposition (muttasila), the judgment of connection (or its negation) is explicit, and any repugnancy is implicit. Thus the definitions remain distinct based on what is judged explicitly.
This resolution is a key logical insight: two propositions can have the same truth conditions but differ in logical form depending on what is explicitly asserted.

Source Text

أي وإن لم يكن الحكم فيها بثبوت شيء لشيء أو نفيه عنه فالقضية شرطية، سواء كان الحكم فيها بثبوت نسبة على تقدير أخرى أو نفي ذلك الثبوت أو بالمنافاة بين النسبتين أو سلب تلك المنافاة، فالأولى شرطية متصلة والثانية شرطية منفصلة. واعلم أن حصر القضية في الحملية والشرطية على ما قرره المصنف عقلي دائر بين النفي والإثبات وأما حصر الشرطية في المتصلة

(عبد)
النسبتين: عنادا أو اتفاقا. أو بالمنافاة : عطف على قوله : ثبوت نسبة، أي سواء كان الحكم في القضية بالمنافاة بين النسبتين، مثل: العدد إما زوج أو فرد وبسلب تلك المنافاة، مثل: ليس العدد إمّا زوجا أو منقسما بمتساويين. The assertions. 4. Names of the parts of the proposition. i.e., if the judgment in it is not the affirmation of something of something or its negation, then the proposition is conditional, whether the judgment in it is the affirmation of a relation upon the supposition of another relation, or the negation of that affirmation, or [the judgment] of mutual repugnancy between the two relations, or the negation of that repugnancy. So the first is a conditional connected [proposition], and the second is a conditional disconnected [proposition]. Know that the division of the proposition into categorical and conditional according to what the author established is rational, revolving between negation and affirmation. As for the division of the conditional into connected [and disconnected]... 'upon the supposition of another' i.e., upon the supposition of another relation, whether the two relations are affirmative or negative or different, as examples will come. (ʿAbd) 'the negation of that affirmation' is a genitive construct attached to 'affirmation of a relation', and it indicates that what is meant by his words 'its negation from it' is the negation of the affirmation of something from something, not the negation of the very thing from something. i.e., if the judgment in the conditional proposition is the negation of the affirmation of a relation upon the supposition of another relation, such as: 'It is not that if the sun is rising, then night exists', and 'It is not that if the sun is not rising, then night does not exist', and 'It is not that if the sun is rising, then day does not exist', and 'It is not that if the sun is not rising, then day exists'. Measure on this the rest of the examples. So according to the first, which is the judgment affirming a relation upon the supposition of another, the conditional proposition is an affirmative connected; and according to the second, which is the judgment negating the affirmation of a relation upon the supposition of another, it is a negative connected. (ʿAbd) For the two relations: opposition or agreement. Or by repugnancy: it is a conjunction attached to his saying 'affirmation of a relation', i.e., whether the judgment in the proposition is of repugnancy between the two relations, like: 'The number is either even or odd', and by negating that repugnancy, like: 'It is not that the number is either even or divisible into two equal parts.' (ʿAbd) 'negation of that repugnancy': an objection was raised against him that the definition of the negative connected applies, i.e., that in which there is a judgment negating the affirmation of a relation upon the supposition of the affirmation of another, to every individual of the positive disconnected; for the judgment in our statement 'The number is either even or odd' is the negation of the affirmation of a relation upon the supposition of another, meaning that it is not that whenever the number is even it is odd. Similarly, the definition of the positive disconnected applies to every individual of the negative connected, for the judgment in our statement 'It is not that whenever the sun is rising, night exists' is nothing but that the two relations are repugnant. And the answer is that what is meant by the negative connected is that in which the negation of connection is judged explicitly, not the more general including explicit and implicit. So the judgment in disconnected propositions is of repugnancy explicitly, whereas the negation of connection is implied. Similarly, what is meant by the positive disconnected is also that in which repugnancy is judged explicitly, and connected propositions are not like that; for the judgment of repugnancy in them is only implied. So understand. (Ismaʿil)
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage defines conditional propositions (al-qadiyya al-shartiyya) and their two subtypes: connected (muttasila) and disconnected (munfasila). The author begins by stating that if a proposition does not simply affirm or deny something of something, it is conditional. In a conditional, the judgment either affirms a relation (nisba) under the supposition of another relation (positive muttasila), negates that affirmation (negative muttasila), judges mutual repugnancy (munafah) between two relations (positive munfasila), or negates that repugnancy (negative munfasila). The commentator expands on these definitions, explaining that 'upon the supposition of another' includes affirmative and negative relations. He then presents an objection: the definition of a negative connected proposition (salb al-ittisal) applies to every individual of the positive disconnected, because in 'the number is either even or odd', the judgment implicitly negates that the number is both even and odd (which is a negation of a relation under a supposition). Conversely, the definition of a positive disconnected applies to the negative connected. The answer distinguishes explicit (sarih) versus implicit (iltizami) judgment: in disconnected propositions, repugnancy is judged explicitly, while negation of connection is only implied; in connected propositions, it is the reverse. Thus the definitions remain distinct. The passage uses examples from natural phenomena (sunrise and night) and arithmetic (even/odd numbers) typical of Islamic logic.

The Non-Temporal Copula in Arabic Logic
Author discusses the Arabic copula: temporal and non-temporal, with borrowed 'huwa' and derived nouns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify temporal and non-temporal copulas in Arabic propositions and explain why 'huwa' was borrowed as a non-temporal linking element.

Core Idea
Arabic borrowed 'huwa' to fill a gap: a non-temporal copula for eternal logical truths.

In this passage, the author and commentator clarify how Arabic logic handles the copula — the link between subject and predicate. While temporal copulas like 'kana' express time-bound relations, Arabic originally lacked a word for timeless linking, needed for propositions about Allah or essences. The solution: borrowing pronouns like 'huwa' or using derived nouns from existential verbs. Let's unpack this linguistic-logical innovation.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish temporal from non-temporal copula
The passage states that temporal copulas are incomplete verbs (e.g., kana). Non-temporal copulas link subject and predicate without implying any time; Arabic needed one for logical propositions. Note the comparison to Persian 'hast' and Greek 'estin'.
2
Examine the borrowing of 'huwa'
The author says Arab logicians borrowed 'huwa' and its variants from pronouns to serve as a non-temporal copula. The commentary clarifies that this borrowing (isti'ara) is lexical, not rhetorical — no similarity required. Also note: 'huwa' becomes a particle when mediating between subject and predicate, according to al-Radi.
3
Identify derived nouns as an alternative
The passage also mentions that nouns derived from incomplete verbs (e.g., ka'in, mawjud) can function as non-temporal copulas. The commentary explains that these nouns negate the temporal accompaniment (iqtiran) inherent in verbs, making them suitable for timeless linking. However, they are rarely used in practice.
4
Follow the commentary's refinement
The commentary specifies that 'incomplete verbs' here means only existential verbs like kana and sara. It also addresses an objection: if derived nouns exist, why borrow 'huwa'? The response: derived nouns are seldom used for linking, so borrowing was more practical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن الرابطة الزمانية في اللغة العربية هي الأفعال الناقصة ولكن لم يجدوا في تلك اللغة رابطة غير زمانية تقوم مقام "هست" في الفارسية و"استن" في اليونانية، فاستعاروا للرابطة الغير الزمانية لفظة "هو" و"هي" ونحوهما مع كونهما في الأصل أسماء لا أدوات. فهذا ما أشار المصنف إليه بقوله: وقد استعير لها هو، وقد يذكر للرابطة الغير الزمانية أسماء مشتقة من الأفعال الناقصة، نحو : "كائن" و "موجود في قولنا: زيد كائن قائما، وأميرس موجود شاعرا. قوله: وإلا فشرطية:
"
الأفعال الناقصة وليس المراد جميعها كما يتراأى من ظاهر هذا الكلام بل المراد الأفعال الوجودية منها كـ"كان وصار" وسائر متصرفاتهما، فاللام على الأفعال للعهد. (عبد) تلك اللغة أي ،العربية أقول قد صرح الرضي وغيره بحرفية "هو" هذا وأخواته المتوسطة بين الموضوع والمحمول فعلى هذا هذه الحكاية إن طابقت الواقع فاستقراء القوم ناقص بعدم ترتيب الكتب العربية وتهذيب مسائل الفنون الأدبية كما يكون الآن، ولتوغلهم في العلوم العقلية وعدم اعتنائهم بغيرها، وإن لم تطابق فاستقراء المعلم الثاني الذي هو الحاكي؛ لانهماكه في النقل من اليونانية إلى اللغة العربية واهتمامه بشأن الترجمة. فاستعاروا والاستعارة ههنا مستعملة في المعنى اللغوي دون الاصطلاحي، فلا یرد أنه لابد في الاستعارة من المناسبة بين المستعار منه وبين المستعار له؛ فإن هذا في الاستعارة الاصطلاحية . ( إسماعيل) وقد يذكر إلخ: دفع لما يتوهم أن من الأسماء المشتقة من الأفعال الناقصة أي الوجودية أيضاً روابط زمانية؛ لأن اسم الفاعل والمفعول أيضاً موضوع للزمان ولهذا قالوا: إنه حقيقة، ولعل وجه ذكرهم إياها للرابطة الغير الزمانية أن المراد بالاقتران في الفعل اقتران الحدث بأحد الأزمنة في الفهم، وسلب هذا الاقتران معتبر في الاسم. فإن قيل: لما وجدوا الرابطة الغير الزمانية أسماء مشتقة من الأفعال الناقصة فلا حاجة لهم إلى الاستعارة بلفظ "هو" ونحوه، قيل: الأسماء المشتقة من الأفعال الوجودية قليل الاستعمال في الربط. (عبد) وأميرس: بضم الأول وفتح الثاني وسكون المثناة التحتانية، اسم ،رجل، ومعنى المثالين: زيد قائم ،ست وأميرس شاعر ست.(عبد)
That the temporal copula in the Arabic language is the incomplete verbs. But they did not find in that language a non-temporal copula similar to 'hast' in Persian and 'estin' in Greek. So they borrowed for the non-temporal copula the word 'huwa' and 'hiya' and their likes, despite them originally being nouns, not particles. So this is what the author indicated by his saying: 'And for it has been borrowed 'huwa'.' And there may be mentioned for the non-temporal copula nouns derived from incomplete verbs, such as 'ka'in' and 'mawjud' in our saying: 'Zayd is (ka'in) standing', and 'Amirs is (mawjud) a poet.' His saying: 'Otherwise it is conditional.' The incomplete verbs, and what is meant is not all of them as appears from the apparent meaning of this speech, but rather the existential verbs from them, like 'kana' and 'sara' and all their conjugations; so the lam on 'verbs' is for [previous] covenant. (ʿAbd) That language, i.e., Arabic. I say: al-Radi and others have stated the particle-ness of 'huwa' and its sisters when intermediate between subject and predicate; so according to this, if this story corresponds to reality, then the induction of the people is deficient due to lack of ordering of Arabic books and refinement of the issues of literary arts as happens now, and due to their engrossment in rational sciences and lack of attention to other sciences. And if it does not correspond, then it is the induction of the second teacher (Aristotle) who narrated it, due to his deep involvement in translating from Greek into Arabic and his concern with translation. So they borrowed; and 'borrowing' (isti'ara) here is used in the lexical meaning, not the technical one, so no objection arises that in [technical] borrowing there must be similarity between the borrowed from and the borrowed for, for this is in technical borrowing. (Ismaʿil) 'And there may be mentioned etc.': repelling what might be supposed that some of the nouns derived from incomplete verbs (i.e., existential ones) are also temporal copulas, because the active and passive participle are also posited for time, and for this they said that it is literal. And perhaps the reason they mentioned them for the non-temporal copula is that what is meant by accompaniment (iqtiran) in the verb is the accompaniment of the event with one of the times in understanding, and the negation of this accompaniment is considered in the noun. For if it is said: Since they found the non-temporal copula [in] nouns derived from incomplete verbs, they have no need to borrow using 'huwa' and its likes. It is answered: The nouns derived from existential verbs are seldom used in linking. (ʿAbd) And 'Amirs': with damm on the first letter, fatha on the second, and sukun on the under-pointed ya; it is the name of a man; and the meaning of the two examples: 'Zayd is standing' and 'Amirs is a poet.' (ʿAbd)
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of the copula (rabita) in Arabic. The author states that the temporal copula (indicating time) is expressed by incomplete verbs like 'kana'. However, Arabic lacks a non-temporal copula equivalent to 'hast' in Persian or 'estin' in Greek. To solve this, Arab logicians borrowed the pronouns 'huwa' and 'hiya' (originally nouns, not particles) to serve as a non-temporal link between subject and predicate. Additionally, derived nouns from incomplete verbs, such as 'ka'in' (being) and 'mawjud' (existent), are used. The commentator expands: 'incomplete verbs' here specifically means existential verbs like 'kana' and 'sara'. He then cites al-Radi, who affirmed that 'huwa' and its variants are particles when they mediate between subject and predicate. The commentator speculates that this story (that the Arabs borrowed these words) may be based on deficient induction due to the neglect of Arabic linguistic arts, or it may reflect Aristotle's own induction as a translator. Importantly, 'isti'ara' (borrowing) here is used in its lexical sense, not the technical rhetorical sense, so no issue of similarity arises. The passage then addresses a potential misunderstanding: one might think that nouns derived from verbs like 'ka'in' are temporal because active/passive participles imply time. The answer clarifies that in these nouns, the temporal accompaniment is negated, making them suitable for non-temporal linking. Finally, a question and answer: why borrow 'huwa' if derived nouns exist? Because derived nouns are rarely used as copulas. The examples given: 'Zayd is standing' (Zaydun ka'inun qa'iman) and 'Amirs is a poet' (Amirsun mawjudun sha'iran), where 'ka'in' and 'mawjud' function as non-temporal copulas.

أفعال ناقصة

Why Disconnected Conditionals Are Also Called Conditional
Author explains the naming of conditional propositions and responds to an objection about munfasila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to rewrite any disconnected conditional proposition as a pair of conditional statements and justify its classification as a conditional.

Core Idea
Even disconnected propositions carry an implicit conditional judgment.

In logic, conditional propositions are named for the presence of a conditional particle. But what about disconnected conditionals like 'The number is either even or odd'? An objector challenges this naming. The author clarifies that the disconnected type is conditional because it implies a hidden 'if-then' structure.

Guided Path
1
Understand the naming criterion
The author states that a proposition is called conditional (shartiyya) if it contains a conditional particle (like 'in').
This is straightforward for muttasila.
2
Identify the objection
The objector notes that this criterion is obvious for connected conditionals but problematic for disconnected ones, which use 'imma' instead of 'in'.
The objection targets the apparent absence of a conditional particle in munfasila.
3
Analyze the answer
The author argues that the disconnected proposition still qualifies because it contains an implicit judgment (hukm dimni) that takes the form of a conditional.
This implicit judgment can be made explicit by rewriting the disjunction as a pair of conditionals.
4
Practise the transformation
Take any disconnected proposition (e.g., 'The number is either even or odd') and rewrite it as a pair of conditionals: 'If even then not odd; if odd then not even.'
This confirms the conditional nature even without an explicit 'if'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فشرطية: إنما سميت بالشرطية لوجود أداة الشرط فيها، يرد عليه أن هذا في المتصلة ظاهر، وأما في المنفصلة فمشكل. والجواب عنه: أن تسمية المفصلة بالشرطية باعتبار خروج حكم ضمني مثلا: معنى قوله: "العدد إما زوج وإما فرد : إن كان العدد فردا فليس بزوج، وإن كان زوجا فليس بفرد.
So 'conditional': it is called conditional because of the presence of the conditional particle in it. An objection is raised against this: that this is apparent in the connected, but as for the disconnected it is problematic. And the answer to it: that the naming of the disconnected as conditional is by virtue of an implicit judgment. For example: the meaning of his statement 'The number is either even or odd' is: If the number is odd, then it is not even; and if it is even, then it is not odd.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This short passage addresses a linguistic point: why the disconnected conditional (munfasila) is also called 'conditional' (shartiyya) when it does not explicitly contain a conditional particle like 'in' (if). The author states that the name comes from the presence of the conditional particle, but an objector points out that this is obvious for the connected conditional (which has 'in' or similar) but problematic for the disconnected (which uses 'imma' (either/or)). The answer is that the disconnected proposition implies a conditional judgment. For example, 'The number is either even or odd' means 'If it is odd, then it is not even; if it is even, then it is not odd.' Thus, the conditional meaning is implicit, justifying the name 'conditional' for both types. This passage illustrates the careful analysis of logical terminology in the Islamic tradition.

حكم ضمني

The Copula and the Borrowing of 'Huwa'
Commentator defines the copula and explains why 'huwa' was borrowed as a non-temporal copula.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the pronoun 'huwa' was borrowed as a non-temporal copula in Arabic logical propositions and distinguish between temporal and non-temporal copulas.

Core Idea
The copula indicates the judgmental relation; when missing, pronouns were borrowed as a non-temporal link.

In Arabic logic, propositions can be bipartite (no explicit copula) or tripartite (with a copula). The standard non-temporal copula is the borrowed pronoun 'huwa'. This passage from the commentary on Tahdhib al-Mantiq defines the copula and explains the historical and linguistic reason for this borrowing, citing al-Fārābī. Let's unpack the logic behind this definition.

Guided Path
1
Define the copula (raabitah)
The commentator explains that the raabitah is the word in a proposition that indicates the judgmental relation (al-nisbah al-hukmiyyah) linking subject and predicate. Note that the term is used for the indicator, but in reality the relation itself is the true copula.
The phrase 'dall ʿalā al-nisbah' shows the copula is an instrument (adāt) for a non-independent meaning.
2
Distinguish tripartite and bipartite propositions
When the copula is mentioned explicitly, the proposition is called tripartite (thulāthiyya); when omitted, it is bipartite (thunāʾiyya). The omission is possible because vowel endings (iʿrāb) can imply the relation by default.
Example: 'Zaydun qāʾimun' (Zayd is standing) is bipartite; 'Zaydun huwa qāʾimun' is tripartite.
3
Identify the division of copulas into temporal and non-temporal
Temporal copulas (like kāna) associate the relation with a specific time (past, present, future). Non-temporal copulas do not. Arabic had temporal copulas but lacked a non-temporal one equivalent to Greek 'esti' or Persian 'hast'.
This distinction is crucial for translating timeless philosophical propositions.
4
Explain the metaphorical borrowing of 'huwa'
Al-Fārābī reports that translators borrowed the pronoun 'huwa' (originally a noun/name) to serve as a non-temporal copula. This is a linguistic metaphor (istiʿāra): transferring a word from its original meaning to a new function. The marginal note addresses the objection that 'huwa' is a name, not an instrument, by clarifying the transfer.
The borrowing solved a practical need: finding a non-temporal link for philosophical discourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محمولا، والدال على النسبة، رابطة، وقد استعير لها "هو".

قوله : محمولا لأنه أمر جعل محمولا لموضوعه قوله والدال على النسبة: أي اللفظة المذكورة في القضية الملفوظة التي تدل على النسبة الحكمية تسمى رابطة تسمية الدال باسم المدلول؛ فإن الرابطة حقيقة هي النسبة الحكمية وفي قوله والدال على النسبة إشارة إلى أن الرابطة أداة؛ لدلالتها على النسبة التي هي معنى حرفي غير مستقل واعلم أن الرابطة قد تذكر في القضية، وقد تحذف فالقضية على الأول تسمى ثلاثيةً وعلى الثاني ثنائية قوله وقد استعير لها "هو": اعلم أن الرابطة تنقسم إلى زمانية تدل على اقتران النسبة الحكمية بأحد الأزمنة الثلاثة، وغير زمانية بخلاف ذلك، وذكر الفارابي أن الحكمة الفلسفية لما نقلت من اللغة اليونانية إلى العربية وجد القوم جعل محمولا وهو قد يكون كلمة، مثل: زيد يقرب، وقد يكون قضية مثل: زيد أبوه قائم، وقد يكون اسما، مثل: كل إنسان حيوان باسم المدلول: الأولى أن يقول بوصف المدلول؛ فإن الرابطة ليست أسماء للنسبة الحكمية، بل إنما هو وصف له إسماعيل النسبة أي على النسبة التي هي مورد الحكم والإذعان؛ لأنه لم يسم اللفظ الدال على نسبة يربط بها الموضوع بالمحمول رابطة ما لم يعتبر معها الوقوع واللاوقوع، ولفظة "هو" رابطة الإيجاب و لم يعتبروا رابطة السلب استغناء بها مع وجود حرف السلب. (عبد) تحذف أي لم تذكر الرابطة في اللفظ اكتفاء عنها بالحركات التي هي علامات دالة عليها بالالتزام لا بالمطابقة كالرفع في الموضوع والمحمول ؛ فإنه دال على كون أحدهما مبتدأ أو محكوما عليه والآخر خبرا ثابتا أو محكوما به، وهذه الدلالة بالالتزام بالمطابقة؛ إذ الإعراب لم يوضع للربط، بل للدلالة على المعاني المعتورة على المعرب، ويلزمها الربط ويفهم منه المعنى الرابطي. استعير: اعلم أن الاستعارة لا بدلها من المستعير والمستعار والمستعار منه المستعير والمستعار والمستعار منه والعجز والافتقار، فشرع في بيان كل منها. فالقوم الناقلون هم المستعيرون والمستعار كلمة "هو" و"هي"، والمستعار منه هو الاسم، وعدم وجدانهم رابطة غير زمانية في كلام العرب عند احتياجهم إليها عجز وافتقار . (عبد) وقد استعير لها هو: جواب عما يقال: إن كون الدال على النسبة الرابطة أداة ممنوع بسند أن "هو" في "زيد" هو قائم" يدل على النسبة، وليس بأداة؛ لأنه اسم فأجاب بقوله: "وقد استعير" "لها" للدلالة على النسبة لفظة "هو" مثلا، يعني أن في أصل الوضع اسم. "هو" ليس بأداة، ثم بالنقل والاستعارة إنه دل على المعنى النسبي التي هي معنى حرفي، فلا ضير. (عبد)
Mahmul (predicate), and that which indicates the relation is called raabitah (copula), and they have metaphorically used 'huwa' for it. His statement: 'mahmul' because it is something made a predicate for its subject. His statement: 'and that which indicates the relation' means the word mentioned in the vocalized proposition that indicates the judgmental relation is called raabitah, naming the indicator after the indicated; for the raabitah in reality is the judgmental relation. In his statement 'and that which indicates the relation' is an indication that the raabitah is an instrument; because it indicates the relation which is a non-independent particle meaning. Know that the raabitah may be mentioned in the proposition, and it may be omitted; so the proposition in the first case is called tripartite, and in the second bipartite. His statement: 'and they have metaphorically used 'huwa' for it': Know that the raabitah is divided into temporal, which indicates the conjunction of the judgmental relation with one of the three times, and non-temporal, which is otherwise. Al-Farabi mentioned that when the philosophical wisdom was transferred from the Greek language to Arabic, the people found that they had made the predicate, which may be a word like 'Zayd is near', or a proposition like 'Zayd, his father is standing', or a noun like 'Every human is an animal' ... by naming the indicator after the indicated: it is better to say 'by describing the indicated'; for the raabitah is not names for the judgmental relation, rather it is a description of it. Ismail: 'the relation' i.e. the relation that is the locus of judgment and affirmation; because the word that indicates a relation by which the subject is linked to the predicate is not called raabitah unless the occurrence and non-occurrence are considered with it. And the word 'huwa' is the affirmative copula, and they did not consider a negative copula, being self-sufficient with the presence of the negative particle. (Abd) 'is omitted' i.e. the raabitah is not mentioned in the utterance, being self-sufficient with the vowel marks that are signs indicating it by implication not by correspondence, like the nominative in the subject and predicate; for it indicates that one of them is the subject or the predicate, and the other is a firmly established predicate. And this indication is by implication, not by correspondence; because declension was not placed for linking, but for indicating the meanings that affect the declined word. The linking is implied by it, and the copulative meaning is understood from it. 'They have metaphorically used': Know that metaphor necessarily has a borrower, a borrowed thing, and a thing borrowed from, and inability and need. He then began to explain each. So the transmitting people are the borrowers, the borrowed thing is the word 'huwa' and 'hiya', and the thing borrowed from is the name. Their not finding a non-temporal copula in Arab speech when they needed it is inability and need. (Abd) 'And they have metaphorically used 'huwa' for it' is a response to what is said: that the claim that the indicator of the relation is a copula is denied on the basis that 'huwa' in 'Zayd is standing' indicates the relation, and it is not an instrument because it is a name. So he answered with his statement: 'they have metaphorically used' 'for it' i.e. for indicating the relation, the word 'huwa' for example, meaning that in its original lexical position 'huwa' is a name, not an instrument. Then by transfer and metaphor it came to indicate the relative meaning which is a particle meaning, so there is no harm. (Abd)
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory commentary on the parts of a proposition, specifically the copula (raabitah). The commentator begins by quoting the matn: 'Mahmul (predicate), and that which indicates the relation is called raabitah, and they have metaphorically used 'huwa' for it.' Then he expounds each part.

First, he explains that 'mahmul' is something made a predicate for its subject.

Second, he defines 'al-dall ala al-nisbah' (that which indicates the relation) as the word in the vocalized proposition that indicates the judgmental relation, and it is called raabitah. He clarifies that we call the indicator by the name of the indicated, i.e., the word is named after the relation it signifies, but in reality the raabitah is the relation itself. He notes that the phrase 'wa al-dall ala al-nisbah' points to the fact that the raabitah is an instrument (adāt) because it indicates a relation which is a non-independent (ghayr mustaqill) meaning, like particles.

Third, he explains that the raabitah may be mentioned explicitly or omitted. If mentioned, the proposition is called tripartite (thulāthiyya); if omitted, it is called bipartite (thunāʾiyya).

Fourth, he turns to the statement 'wa qad istaʿīra la-hā huwa' (and they have metaphorically used 'huwa' for it). He explains that the raabitah is divided into temporal (zamāniyya) and non-temporal (ghayr zamāniyya). The temporal copula indicates the conjunction of the judgmental relation with one of the three times (past, present, future). The non-temporal copula does not. He then cites al-Fārābī's account: when the philosophical wisdom was translated from Greek into Arabic, the translators needed a non-temporal copula equivalent to 'esti' in Greek and 'hast' in Persian. They found that Arabic had temporal copulas (imperfect verbs like kāna) but no non-temporal copula. Therefore, they borrowed the pronouns 'huwa' and 'hiya' for this purpose, although these are originally names, not particles. This is a metaphorical use (istiʿāra) in the linguistic sense, not the rhetorical term, meaning they transferred the word from its original use to a new one.

The commentator then addresses an objection: if 'huwa' is a name, how can it be an instrument? The answer is that in its original lexical position it is a name, but by transfer and metaphorical use it came to indicate the relative meaning which is a particle meaning, so there is no problem.

The passage uses examples from Arabic: 'Zayd is standing', 'Zayd's father is standing', 'Every human is an animal'. These are from the Islamic sciences, illustrating logic with common linguistic structures.

Key Terms
الرابطة
استعارة
هو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the early logicians borrow the word 'huwa' as a non-temporal copula in Arabic propositional logic?
A) Because Arabic already had a non-temporal copula, but it was rarely used.
B) Because they could not find a non-temporal copula in Arabic equivalent to the Greek 'esti' and Persian 'hast', so they metaphorically transferred the pronoun 'huwa'.
C) Because 'huwa' was originally a particle indicating relation.
D) Because they wanted to imitate Greek syntax exactly.

Key Takeaway
The copula (raabitah) is the indicator of the judgmental relation; Arabic lacked a non-temporal copula, so translators borrowed the pronoun 'huwa' as a linguistic metaphor, turning a noun into an instrument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Definition of Proposition in Islamic Logic
Author defines proposition and its categorical types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia in the definition of a proposition and classify any categorical proposition as affirmative or negative.

Core Idea
The proposition is a saying that can be true or false – it is the basic unit of assent.

Before studying arguments (hujaj) in logic, you must first understand their building blocks: propositions (qadaya). In this passage, the author defines the proposition and explains its two fundamental types: affirmative and negative categoricals. He also clarifies that the term 'saying' (qawl) includes both mental and verbal composites, and defines truth and falsehood in terms of correspondence to reality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of proposition
Extract the two essential conditions: it must be a saying (qawl) and it must admit truth and falsehood.
2
Distinguish the two divisions of categorical propositions
Note whether the judgment affirms or denies something of something to classify as affirmative (mujiba) or negative (saliba).
3
Define subject and predicate
The subject (mawdu') is what is judged upon; the predicate (mahmul) is what is judged of it.
4
Clarify the term 'qawl'
Understand that in logic, 'saying' includes both conceptual and verbal composites, so the definition covers mental as well as spoken propositions.
This prevents misunderstanding from the common linguistic usage.
5
Apply the definition to an Islamic proposition
Test a sample proposition (e.g., 'Prayer is obligatory') to see if it satisfies the definition and determine its type.

فصل في التصديقات القضية قول يحتمل الصدق والكذب، فإن كان الحكم فيها بثبوت شيء لشيء أو نفيه عنه فحملية موجبة أو سالبة، ويسمى المحكوم عليه موضوعا والمحكوم به قوله: القضية قول القول في عرف هذا الفن يقال للمركب، سواء كان مركبا معقولا أو ملفوظا، فالتعريف يشمل القضية المعقولة والملفوظة. قوله: يحتمل الصدق والكذب: الصدق هو المطابقة للواقع، والكذب هو اللامطابقة له، وهذا المعنى لا يتوقف معرفته على معرفة الخبر والقضية، فلا يلزم الدور. قوله موضوعاً: لأنه وُضِعَ وعيّن ليحكم عليه. القضية إلخ: وأنت تعلم أن المقصود في المنطق معرفة الموصل إلى التصور وهو المعرف، ومعرفة الموصل إلى التصديق وهو الحجة. فلما فرغ عن بيان الأول شرع في بيان الثاني، وهو قسمان :قريب وهو الحجة؛ لأنه الموصل إلى التصديق بلا واسطة وبعيد وهو القضية؛ لكونها جزء الحجة، وقدمها لتوقف القريب عليها؛ لتركبه منها. (عبد) الصدق بالنظر إلى مفهومها مع قطع النظر عن خصوصية الموضوع والمحمول وغير ذلك، فلا يرد أن القضايا البديهية الأولية كاجتماع النقيضين محال لا تحتمل الكذب والقضايا التي يحكم العقل بكذبها كـ"السماء تحتنا" لا تحتمل الصدق . ( إسماعيل) وهذا المعنى إلخ: دفع للاعتراض المشهور على تعريف القضية بلزوم الدور بأن الصدق والكذب مطابقة الخبر للواقع وعدم مطابقة له والخبر والقضية مترادفان، فتوقفت القضية على الصدق والكذب المتوقفين على الخبر، وهذا هو الدور، وحاصل الدفع أن معرفتهما بنفس المطابقة للواقع واللامطابقة له، فلا دور. فإن قيل: فاعل المطابقة واللامطابقة إنما هو الخبر، فالدور باق، قلنا: لا نسلم الحصر لجريانهما في غير الخبر أيضاً؛ فإن التصور قد يطابق الصورة وقد لا يطابقه كما بيّن، وإن سلمناه فنقول: فاعلها في الحقيقة النسبة؛ لأن المطابقة أولا وبالذات للنسبة وثانيا وبالعرض للخبر؛ لاشتماله عليها، ويعلم من تعريف القضية أنها لابد وأن يكون حكاية عن المحكي عنه. (عبد)
Section on assents. The proposition is a saying that admits truth and falsehood. If the judgment in it is about affirming something for something or negating it, then it is categorical affirmative or negative. The subject of judgment is called subject and what is judged is called predicate. He said: 'The proposition is a saying.' The term 'saying' in the custom of this art is used for a composite, whether it is a conceptual composite or a verbal composite, so the definition includes both conceptual and verbal propositions. He said: 'admits truth and falsehood.' Truth is correspondence with reality, and falsehood is non-correspondence with it. This meaning does not require knowledge of the report and the proposition, so there is no circularity. He said: 'subject' because it is placed and designated to be judged upon. The proposition etc.: And you know that the purpose in logic is knowledge of what leads to conception, which is the definition, and knowledge of what leads to assent, which is the argument. When he finished explaining the first, he began explaining the second, and it is two divisions: immediate, which is the argument because it leads to assent without intermediary, and remote, which is the proposition because it is a part of the argument, and he presented it because the immediate depends on it since it is composed from it. (Abd) 'The saying': a refutation of what is imagined from the fame of 'saying' in verbal that the definition of proposition by this is specific to the verbal proposition, meaning yes! that 'saying' by language is specific to the verbal, and therefore it is famous in it, but in the terminology of logicians it includes the verbal and the conceptual. (Abd) 'Truth' with regard to its concept, disregarding the particularity of subject and predicate and otherwise, so it does not apply that the primary self-evident propositions like the conjunction of two contradictories are impossible and do not admit falsehood, and propositions that the mind judges as false like 'the sky is beneath us' do not admit truth. (Ismail) And this meaning etc.: a refutation of the famous objection to the definition of proposition entailing circularity, that truth and falsehood are correspondence of the report to reality and non-correspondence, and report and proposition are synonymous, so the proposition depends on truth and falsehood which depend on the report, and this is circularity. The summary of the refutation is that their knowledge is by the same correspondence to reality and non-correspondence, so no circularity. If it is said: the agent of correspondence and non-correspondence is only the report, so circularity remains, we say: we do not concede the exclusivity because they apply to other than the report as well; for conception may correspond to the form and may not correspond as was explained, and if we concede it then we say: the agent of them in reality is the relation, because correspondence first and intrinsically is for the relation, and second and accidentally for the report due to its inclusion of it, and it is known from the definition of proposition that it must be a report about the reported-about. (Abd)
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage defines the proposition (qadiyya) and explains its essential nature and divisions. The author begins by stating that the proposition is a saying that admits truth and falsehood. He then explains that if the judgment involves affirming or negating something of something, it is a categorical proposition, either affirmative or negative. The thing judged about is called the subject, and what is judged is called the predicate. He then clarifies key terms: 'saying' (qawl) in logic refers to any composite, whether mental or verbal, so the definition includes both mental and spoken propositions. 'Truth' (sidq) is correspondence to reality, and 'falsehood' is lack of correspondence. The author defends the definition against the charge of circularity by arguing that the meaning of truth and falsehood does not depend on prior knowledge of khabar or proposition. He further explains that the subject is called subject because it is what the judgment is placed upon. The passage also discusses the purpose of logic: to reach conception (tasawwur) through definitions and assent (tasdiq) through arguments. It distinguishes between immediate (the argument itself) and remote (the proposition) means to assent. Finally, it addresses potential objections, such as the claim that self-evident propositions cannot admit falsehood, and provides a nuanced response. The key terms used in the definition are carefully defined to prevent misunderstandings.

Propositions

Defending the Definition: No Circularity in the Proposition
Author refutes circularity objection and explains purpose of proposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the circularity objection against the definition of proposition and explain the author's refutation.

Core Idea
Truth and falsehood are known by direct correspondence, not by prior knowledge of propositions – so no circularity.

A common objection to defining the proposition as 'a saying that admits truth and falsehood' is that truth and falsehood themselves depend on the proposition (or report), creating a vicious circle. The author refutes this by arguing that truth and falsehood are simply correspondence to reality, and this correspondence is known without needing the proposition. He also explains the ultimate purpose of studying propositions: they are the remote means to assent, while arguments are the immediate means.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection (دور)
The objection claims that truth and falsehood are defined via the report (khabar), which is synonymous with proposition, leading to circularity.
The objection: proposition depends on truth/falsehood, which depend on khabar, which is the same as proposition.
2
Extract the author's first response
The meaning of truth and falsehood is known directly from correspondence to reality, independent of knowledge of report or proposition.
3
Examine the second response
Even if truth/falsehood apply to khabar, they apply primarily to the relation (nisba) within the proposition, not to the whole proposition.
4
Understand the broader context
The proposition is a 'remote' means to assent, while the argument (hujja) is the immediate means. Study of propositions is necessary because arguments are composed of them.
