Matter and Modality of Propositions
Defines matter and modality, distinguishes qualified from absolute propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the matter (madda) and modality (jiha) of a proposition, and identify whether a modal proposition is qualified or absolute.

Core Idea
The truth of a modal proposition depends on the match between its stated modality and the actual matter in reality.

When we evaluate propositions that include modal terms like 'necessarily' or 'possibly', we must separate the objective modal relation between subject and predicate (the matter) from the linguistic expression of that relation (the modality). This lesson clarifies these two fundamental concepts and explains how they determine truth or falsehood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal structure
Find the subject, predicate, and any explicit modal expression (e.g., 'necessarily', 'always', 'possibly') in the proposition.
If no modal word appears, the proposition is absolute (mutlaqa).
2
Determine the matter (madda)
Ask: What is the actual modal relation between subject and predicate in reality? Is it necessary, possible, or impossible? This is the matter.
The matter is independent of the proposition's wording.
3
Distinguish the modality (jiha)
Identify the word or phrase that indicates the modality in the spoken or mental proposition. This is the modality.
Example: 'necessarily' is a jiha if explicitly stated.
4
Check correspondence for truth
If the stated modality matches the actual matter, the proposition is true; otherwise it is false.
A mismatch makes the proposition false, regardless of the subject-predicate connection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإلا فمطلقة فإن كان الحكم فيها بضرورة النسبة ما دام ذات الموضوع موجودة فضرورية تسمى مادة القضية ثم قد يصرح في القضية بأن تلك النسبة مكيفية في نفس الأمر بكيفية كذا فالقضية حينئذ تسمى موجبة، وقد لا يصرح بذلك فتسمى القضية مطلقة. واللفظ الدال عليها في القضية الملفوظة والصورة العقلية الدالة عليها في القضية المعقولة يسمى جهة القضية. فإن طابقت الجهة المادة صدقت القضية كقولنا كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة وإلا كذبت كقولنا: كل إنسان بالضرورة.
Otherwise it is absolute. If the judgment in it is about the necessity of the predication as long as the essence of the subject exists, it is necessary. [The modality in reality] is called the matter of the proposition. Then sometimes in the proposition it is specified that that predication is qualified in reality with a certain modality, so the proposition is then called qualified, and sometimes it is not specified, so the proposition is called absolute. And the word indicating it in the spoken proposition and the mental form indicating it in the mental proposition is called the modality of the proposition. So if the modality matches the matter, the proposition is true, like our saying 'Every human is an animal necessarily', otherwise it is false, like our saying 'Every human is a stone necessarily'.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage defines two crucial concepts: matter (madda) and modality (jiha) of a proposition. The matter is the actual modal state of the predication in reality—whether it is necessary, possible, or impossible. For example, the actual relation between 'human' and 'animal' is one of necessity, so the matter is necessity. The modality (jiha) is the linguistic expression or mental form that indicates this matter in the proposition. When the speaker explicitly states the modality (e.g., 'necessarily', 'always'), the proposition is called qualified (muwajjaha). When the modality is not stated, it is absolute (mutlaqa). The truth of a qualified proposition depends on whether the stated modality matches the actual matter. For example, 'Every human is an animal necessarily' is true because the actual matter is necessity. But 'Every human is a stone necessarily' is false because the actual matter is impossibility, not necessity. This distinction is essential for evaluating modal propositions.

Key Terms
مادة القضية
جهة القضية
موجبة
مطلقة
ضرورية

D) No human is a stone. (لا إنسان حجر)

Key Takeaway
The matter is the actual modality in reality; the modality is the expression; truth requires that the modality matches the matter.

The Truth Condition of Modal Propositions: Matter and Modality
Explains the truth condition of modal propositions via matter and modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the matter (madda) and modality (jiha) in any modal proposition and verify whether the stated modality is compatible with the actual matter.

Core Idea
The truth of a modal proposition requires compatibility between its stated modality and the actual modality in reality.

Modal propositions express more than just predication; they qualify it with necessity, possibility, or impossibility. How do we judge their truth? The key is the concept of 'matter' (madda) – the actual modality in the world – and 'modality' (jiha) – the verbal expression. This lesson explains their relationship and the condition for truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject, predicate, and stated modality (jiha).
In a modal proposition like 'Every human is necessarily an animal', the jiha is 'necessarily'. Note the subject and predicate.
The jiha is the explicit modal term.
2
Determine the actual modality (madda) of the predication in reality.
The madda is the actual state of the predication. For 'human is animal', the madda is necessity because it is always true.
Use your knowledge of the world or the discipline (e.g., kalam) to assess.
3
Compare jiha and madda for compatibility.
If the jiha is general possibility (al-imkan al-‘amm), it includes necessity, so it is compatible. If it is specific possibility (al-imkan al-khass), it would exclude necessity and thus be incompatible.
General possibility means 'not impossible', which includes necessity.
4
Apply to examples.
'Every human is a writer necessarily' is false because the jiha (necessity) is incompatible with the madda (possibility). 'Every human is a stone necessarily' is false due to contradictory predication, but also the jiha doesn't match.
The truth condition is about modality match, not just factual truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : فإن كان الحكم فيها بضرورة النسبة إلخ أي قد يكون الحكم في القضية الموجهة بأن النسبة الثبوتية أو السلبية ضرورية . مادة القضية: اعلم أن المادة لفظ مشترك بين الطرفين والكيفية الثابتة في نفس الأمر؛ لأن كلا منهما جزء القضية المرابة وعنصرها وقال بعضهم: إن مادة القضية هي كيفية النسبة في نفس الأمر، وإليه ذهب الشارح اليزدي، وإنما سميت تلك الكيفية بمادة القضية؛ لأن مادة الشيء هي ما يتركب عنه ويكون أصلا لها، فمادة القضية أصلها وهي الموضوع والمحمول والنسبة ولكن أشرف هذه الأجزاء الثلاثة هو النسبة وتلك الكيفية الثابتة في نفس الأمر لازمة لها، فسميت تلك الكيفية مادة تسمية اللازم للجزء الأشرف باسم الكل. وأيضاً لما كان مدار صدق القضية وكذبها على مطابقة الجهة لكيفية الثابتة في نفس الأمر عدمها صار تلك الكيفية بمنزلة المادة فأطلق اسم المادة عليها إصلاحا. جهة القضية لأنها تدل على جهة النسبة ،وحالها، فالفرق بين الجهة والمادة أن الأول دال والثاني ،مدلول وبعضهم صرحوا بأن جهة القضية هي تلك الكيفية فتسمية الدال عليها جهة من باب تسمية الدال باسم المدلول. وإلا كذبت فإن قلت إن الجهة قد تكون غير مطابقة للمادة والقضية صادقة، نحو كل إنسان حيوان بالإمكان العام؛ فإن المادة مادة الضرورة. قلت الإمكان العام أعم من الضرورة، فالجهة مطابقة للمادة. بمعنى أنه ليس مباينا لها . إسماعيل) :حجر لو قال كل إنسان كاتب بالضرورة لكان أولى؛ لأن كذبه ليس إلا بعدم مطابقة الجهة المذكورة فيه للمادة بخلاف كل إنسان حجر بالضرورة؛ فإن كذبه لمخالفة النسبة لكيفية النفس الأمرية، كما لا يخفى. His saying: 'If the judgment in it is about necessity of the predication, etc.' means that the judgment in the qualified proposition may be that the affirmative or negative predication is necessary. Commentary
This passage expands on the previous definitions. It provides a detailed commentary on the matter (madda) and modality (jiha). The commentator explains that the term 'matter' is used ambiguously: sometimes it refers to the subject and predicate, but here it specifically refers to the actual modality in reality. The reason for calling it 'matter' is that just as matter is the basic constituent of a thing, here the actual modality is the basis for the truth of the proposition. The modality (jiha) is the indicator of that matter. The truth condition is stated: the stated modality must match the actual matter. A potential objection is raised: what if the modality is general possibility (al-imkan al-‘amm) while the matter is necessity? For example, 'Every human is an animal possibly in the general sense' is true even though the matter is necessity. The answer is that general possibility includes necessity, so there is no mismatch; the modality is not opposite to the matter. The passage then classifies qualified propositions into simple (basita) and compound (murakkaba). A simple qualified proposition consists of only one modality (either affirmative or negative), while a compound one is composed of both affirmation and negation in its very concept. An example of a compound would be a conditional proposition that includes both a condition and a result? Actually here it likely refers to modal propositions that combine possibility and necessity? The text says a compound is 'composed of affirmation and negation', for example, a proposition like 'Every human is necessarily an animal not a stone'? But that seems contrived. Possibly it refers to modals that have both a positive and negative aspect. The author prioritizes simple propositions because they are more fundamental.

Classification of Modal Propositions into Simple and Compound
Classifies modal propositions into simple and compound.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a modal proposition as simple (basita) or compound (murakkaba) based on its conceptual composition.

Core Idea
Simple modal propositions assert only one modality; compound ones combine both affirmation and negation in their very concept.

Modal propositions can be divided based on their internal logical structure. A simple modal proposition consists solely of either an affirmation or a negation with a single modality. A compound proposition integrates both affirmation and negation into its meaning. Understanding this division helps in analyzing the logical form of modal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify whether the proposition's concept involves both affirmation and negation.
Simple: the concept is purely affirmative or purely negative. Compound: the concept is composed of both. For example, 'Every human is necessarily an animal' is simple; 'Every human is necessarily an animal and not possibly a non-animal' is compound.
The compound does not mean a conjunction of two propositions; it is a single modal proposition with a mixed concept.
2
Check if the modal qualifier itself indicates a blend.
If the modality explicitly includes both affirmation and negation in its meaning (e.g., 'necessarily not' is still simple negation; but 'necessarily and not possibly' combines two modalities), it may be compound.
The text says compound is 'composed of affirmation and negation' in its concept.
3
Apply the classification to examples.
'Every human is possibly a writer' – simple affirmative. 'Every human is necessarily not a stone' – simple negative. 'Every human is necessarily an animal and not possibly a non-animal' – compound.
The author presents simple ones first because they are more fundamental.

Also, since the truth and falsehood of the proposition depend on the match between the modality and the fixed modality in reality or its absence, that modality becomes like the material, so the name 'matter' is applied to it figuratively. The difference between modality and matter is that the first is the indicator and the second is the indicated. 'Otherwise it is false.' For if you say that the modality may not match the matter yet the proposition is true, like 'Every human is an animal possibly in the general sense' while the matter is necessity, I say that general possibility is more general than necessity, so the modality matches the matter in the sense that it is not opposite to it. [Abdul]: 'If the judgment is, etc.' Then the qualified proposition is either simple or compound. The simple is that whose reality is either only affirmation or only negation. Commentary
This passage expands on the previous definitions. It provides a detailed commentary on the matter (madda) and modality (jiha). The commentator explains that the term 'matter' is used ambiguously: sometimes it refers to the subject and predicate, but here it specifically refers to the actual modality in reality. The reason for calling it 'matter' is that just as matter is the basic constituent of a thing, here the actual modality is the basis for the truth of the proposition. The modality (jiha) is the indicator of that matter. The truth condition is stated: the stated modality must match the actual matter. A potential objection is raised: what if the modality is general possibility (al-imkan al-‘amm) while the matter is necessity? For example, 'Every human is an animal possibly in the general sense' is true even though the matter is necessity. The answer is that general possibility includes necessity, so there is no mismatch; the modality is not opposite to the matter. The passage then classifies qualified propositions into simple (basita) and compound (murakkaba). A simple qualified proposition consists of only one modality (either affirmative or negative), while a compound one is composed of both affirmation and negation in its very concept. An example of a compound would be a conditional proposition that includes both a condition and a result? Actually here it likely refers to modal propositions that combine possibility and necessity? The text says a compound is 'composed of affirmation and negation', for example, a proposition like 'Every human is necessarily an animal not a stone'? But that seems contrived. Possibly it refers to modals that have both a positive and negative aspect. The author prioritizes simple propositions because they are more fundamental.

Distinguishing Perpetual and Conventional Propositions
Author defines perpetual and conventional propositions with example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given proposition is perpetual (dā’ima) or conventional (‘urfiyya) based on whether perpetuity is qualified by a descriptive attribute.

Core Idea
Perpetual propositions have unqualified perpetuity; conventional ones tie perpetuity to a descriptive attribute.

In modal logic, propositions are classified by how their judgment relates to time. The author defines two key types: perpetual (dā’ima) and conventional (‘urfiyya). Understanding the difference is essential for correctly interpreting statements that involve continuous judgment, especially when the judgment depends on a changing attribute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining feature of a perpetual proposition
The perpetual proposition (dā’ima) conveys a judgment that continuously holds without any restriction by a descriptive attribute. For example, 'All humans are animals' is perpetual if understood as always true regardless of any additional description.
The term 'perpetual' comes from the inclusion of perpetuity (dawām).
2
Identify the defining feature of a conventional proposition
The conventional proposition (‘urfiyya) holds perpetually but only as long as a certain descriptive attribute (waṣf ‘unwānī) remains attached to the subject. The example given is 'Every writer moves his fingers': the movement holds as long as the person is a writer.
The name 'conventional' comes from common usage (al-‘urf) understanding this meaning.
3
Distinguish using the example from the passage
Take the proposition 'Every writer moves his fingers'. Is it perpetual or conventional? The passage says common people understand it as conventional: the judgment holds as long as the subject is a writer. If it were perpetual, it would mean the movement holds always, even when not writing — which is false.
This example clarifies the role of the descriptive attribute 'writer'.
4
Relate to the absolute general proposition
The absolute general (muṭlaqa ‘āmma) is defined as a judgment that the relation is actually realized, without any temporal or modal qualification. It is more general than the conventional and serves as a background for further modal distinctions.
The passage notes that the conventional is a subtype of the absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سميت القضية دائمة؛ لاشتمالها على الدوام، ومطلقة؛ لعدم تقييد الدوام بالوصف العنواني أو إن كان الحكم بالدوام الوصفي أي بعدم انفكاك النسبة عن ذات الموضوع ما دام الوصف العنواني ثابتا لتلك الذات سميت عرفية؛ لأن أهل العرف يفهمون هذا المعنى من القضية السالبة بل من الموجبة أيضاً عند الإطلاق، فإذا قيل: كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع فهموا أن هذا الحكم ثابت ما دام كاتبا، وعامة؛ لكونها أعم من العرفية الخاصة التي سيجيء ذكرها. قوله: أو بفعليتها: أي بتحقق النسبة بالفعل. فالمطلقة العامة هي التي حكم فيها بكون النسبة متحققة
The proposition is called 'perpetual' because it includes perpetuity, and 'absolute' because perpetuity is not restricted by a descriptive attribute; or if the judgment is with descriptive perpetuity, i.e., the relation is never separated from the essence of the subject as long as the descriptive attribute is established for that essence, it is called 'conventional' because the common people understand this meaning from the negative proposition, and also from the affirmative when unqualified. So when it is said: 'Every writer is moving his fingers,' they understand that this judgment is established as long as he is a writer. And 'general' because it is more general than the particular conventional which will be mentioned. His saying: 'or by its actuality' means by the actual realization of the relation. So the absolute general is that in which judgment is made that the relation is realized.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage defines two types of modal propositions: perpetual (al-dā’ima) and conventional (al-‘urfiyya). The perpetual proposition is one that expresses a judgment with perpetual continuity (al-dawām) without any qualification by a descriptive attribute. In contrast, the conventional proposition ties the perpetuity to a descriptive attribute (al-waṣf al-‘unwānī): the relation holds for the subject as long as that attribute persists. For example, 'Every writer moves his fingers' is understood by common people (ahl al-‘urf) to mean that the movement is constant as long as the person is writing. The commentator then clarifies that 'by its actuality' (bi-fi‘liyyatihā) refers to the actual realization of the relation, and the absolute general (al-muṭlaqa al-‘āmma) is defined as a judgment that the relation actually holds, without further modal qualification.

Definition of the Absolute General Proposition
Author defines absolute general proposition as actualized relation in one of the three times.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the absolute general proposition and distinguish it from modal propositions using its defining features.

Core Idea
The absolute general proposition is the default unqualified judgment of actuality at some time.

This passage introduces a fundamental type of proposition in Islamic logic: the absolute general (al-muṭlaqa al-‘āmma). Understanding this definition is essential because it serves as the baseline against which all modal restrictions—necessity, perpetuity, and others—are understood. We will unpack the two parts of its name to see what they reveal about the proposition's nature and scope.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Locate the term being defined: 'absolute general proposition' (al-muṭlaqa al-‘āmma). Note that the author defines it by stating a judgment (ḥukm) about a relation (nisba) being actualized.
The definiendum is a type of proposition (qadiyya), not a term.
2
Extract the defining characteristic
The defining property is that the relation is actualized (mutaḥaqqiqa bi’l-fi‘l) in one of the three times (past, present, or future). This means the proposition asserts actual occurrence, not mere possibility or necessity.
'Actualized' (bi’l-fi‘l) contrasts with potential (bi’l-quwwa).
3
Understand why it is called 'absolute'
The author explains it is called 'absolute' (muṭlaqa) because when a proposition is uttered without any qualifier (like 'necessarily' or 'always'), the default understanding is that the relation holds at some time. The term 'absolute' reflects this lack of modal restriction.
In later texts, 'muṭlaqa' may be used more broadly, but here it is technical.
4
Understand why it is called 'general'
It is called 'general' (‘āmma) because it is more general than the existential non-perpetual (al-wujūdiyya lā dā’ima) and the non-necessary (lā ḍarūriyya) that will be discussed later. This generality means it covers a wider range of propositions.
The contrast with later types shows its broader scope.
5
Contrast with modal propositions
Notice that the absolute general proposition is defined in opposition to those restricted by necessity (ḍarūra) or perpetuity (dawām). The absence of such restrictions is what makes it 'absolute'.
This contrast is key to understanding the entire classification of propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالمطلقة العامة هي التي حكم فيها بكون النسبة متحققة بالفع أي في أحد الأزمنة الثلاثة وتسميتها بالمطلقة؛ لأن هذا هو المفهوم من القضية عند إطلاقها وعدم تقييدها بالضرورة أو بالدوام أو غير ذلك من الجهات وبالعامة؛ لكونها أعم من الوجودية اللادائمة واللاضرورية على ما سيجيء.
So the absolute general proposition is that in which judgment is made that the relation is actualized, i.e., in one of the three times. It is called 'absolute' because this is the meaning understood from the proposition when it is unqualified and not restricted by necessity, perpetuity, or other modalities; and 'general' because it is more general than the existential non-perpetual and the non-necessary, as will come.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage defines the absolute general proposition (al-muṭlaqa al-‘āmma). It is a judgment that the relation (al-nisba) is actually realized (mutḥaqqiqa bi’l-fi‘l) in one of the three times: past, present, or future. It is called 'absolute' (muṭlaqa) because when a proposition is stated without any modal qualifier (like necessity or perpetuity), the default understanding is that the relation holds at some time. It is called 'general' (‘āmma) because it is broader in scope than the existential non-perpetual (al-wujūdiyya lā dā’ima) and the non-necessary (lā ḍarūriyya) that will be discussed later. The commentator clarifies that 'actualized' means actually occurring, not merely potential.

Key Takeaway

Definition of the General Possible Proposition
Defines general possible as absence of necessity of contradictory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the general possible proposition (al-mumkina al-‘āmma) in terms of the negation of necessity of its contradictory.

Core Idea
The general possible proposition is defined solely by what is not necessary.

The author defines a new type of proposition not by what it asserts, but by what it refrains from asserting. Unlike absolute propositions that affirm or deny a relation, the general possible proposition only tells us that the opposite is not necessary. This negative definition captures possibility without requiring actual occurrence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining condition
Find the phrase 'or by the absence of necessity of its contradictory'. This is the core: the proposition asserts that the opposite of the stated relation is not necessary.
The contradictory depends on whether the proposition is affirmative or negative.
2
Apply the definition to an example
Consider 'Zayd is a writer with general possibility'. This does not say that Zayd actually writes, but that writing is not impossible for him. The negation of 'Zayd is a writer' (i.e., 'Zayd is not a writer') is not necessary.
3
Distinguish from other modalities
Contrast with 'Zayd is a writer necessarily' (ḍarūriyya) or 'Zayd is a writer actually' (fi‘liyya). The general possible does not assert actuality or necessity.
4
Identify the role of negation
Notice that the definition uses a double negation: the opposite is not necessary. In an affirmative proposition, possibility means its negation is not necessary; in a negative proposition, possibility means its affirmation is not necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله أو بعدم ضرورة خلافها: أي إذا حكم في القضية بأن خلاف النسبة المذكورة فيها ليس ضروريا نحو قولنا زيد كاتب بالإمكان العام يعني أن الكتابة غير مستحيلة له بمعنى أن سلبها عنه ليس بضروري سميت القضية حينئذ ممكنة؛ لاشتمالها على الإمكان وهو سلب الضرورة، وعامة؛ كما أنه بالفع أي في أحد الأزمنة الثلاثة. فيه نظر: فإن تفسير "بالفعل" بأحد الأزمنة الثلاثة باطل؛ فإنه يخرج من تعريف المطلقة العامة التي يكون موضوعها بريئاً من الزمان، كقولنا: الواجب قديم وغير ذلك مع أنها داخلة تحتها على لا يبقى حينئذ فرق بينها وبين المنتشرة والمطلقة كما لا يخفى بل المراد بالفعل ما يقابل القوة فالصواب أن يقال بدل قوله : أي في أحد الأزمنة الثلاثة "أي في الجملة" كما يظهر من كلام المصنف أيضاً في بعض تصانيفه.(إسماعيل) هذا أي كون النسبة متحققة بالفعل المفهوم من القضية فلما كان هذا المعنى مفهوم القضية المطلقة سميت بها. أعم من الوجودية إلخ لأن الأولى : عبارة عن المطلقة العامة المقيدة باللادوام الذاتي والثانية: باللاضرورة الذاتية خلاف النسبة المذكورة: فإن كانت القضية إيجابية فخلافها سلبية، فالإمكان في الموجبة بمعنى سيجيء. أن سلبها ليس ضروريا، وإن كانت سالبة فخلافها إيجابية، فالإمكان في السالبة بمعنى أن إيجابها ليس ضروريا. (برهان :ممكنة ومحمولها أيضاً عرض مفارق؛ لأنها أعم من الفعلية والفعلية أعم القضايا وأعم الأعم أعم؛ لأنها أعم من سائر القضايا فتحقق في مواد سائر القضايا ولا عكس. ممكنة ذهب بعضهم إلى أن الممكنة العامة ليست قضية حقيقة؛ فإن القضية لابد فيها من الحكم أي الوقوع واللاوقوع، ولا شك أنه غير مشتملة عليه، وإذا لم تكن قضية فكيف تكون موجبة؛ فإن الموجبة قسم من القضية وقد نقضه بعضهم بأن ذلك خطأ؛ لأن الحكم أي الوقوع عبارة عن نفس الثبوت أعم من أن يكون على نهج الفعلية أو لا، فالإمكان كيفية زائدة على الحكم غاية الأمر أن المتبادر من الحكم الفعلية، والإمكان أضعف المدارج لكنه لا يضر في العمومية كما أن الوجود أعم شامل للموجود الخارجي والذهني مع أن المتبادر هو الوجود الخارجي، والوجود الذهني أضعف من الخارجي كما تقرر عند الحكماء. إسماعيل)
His saying: 'or by the absence of necessity of its contradictory' means: if it is judged in the proposition that the opposite of the mentioned relation is not necessary, like our saying: 'Zayd is a writer with general possibility', meaning that writing is not impossible for him, i.e., its negation is not necessary. Then the proposition is called 'possible' because it includes possibility, which is the negation of necessity; and 'general'; as it is by actuality i.e., in one of the three times. There is a consideration: for interpreting 'by actuality' as one of the three times is invalid; because it would exclude from the definition of absolute general propositions whose subject is free from time, like our saying: 'The Necessary Existent is eternal' and others, even though they are included under it, and then there would be no difference between it and the widespread and absolute as is clear. But the intended meaning of 'by actuality' is what is opposite to potentiality, so the correct thing is to say instead of his saying 'i.e., in one of the three times' 'i.e., in general', as appears from the author's words also in some of his works. (Isma‘il) This, i.e., that the relation is actualized, is what is understood from the proposition. Since this meaning is the meaning of the absolute proposition, it is named by it. More general than the existential etc., because the first is the absolute general restricted by the lack of essential perpetuity and the second by the lack of essential necessity. The opposite of the mentioned relation: if the proposition is affirmative, its opposite is negative, so possibility in the affirmative means that its negation is not necessary; if it is negative, its opposite is affirmative, so possibility in the negative means that its affirmation is not necessary. (Burhan) Possible: and its predicate is also a separable accident because it is more general than the actual, and the actual is the most general of propositions, and the most general of the most general is the most general because it is more general than other propositions, so it is realized in the matters of other propositions and not vice versa. Possible: some went to the view that the general possible is not a real proposition; for a proposition necessarily includes judgment, i.e., occurrence or non-occurrence, and there is no doubt that it does not include it. If it is not a proposition, how can it be affirmative? For the affirmative is a division of the proposition. Some refuted this by saying that is an error; because judgment (i.e., occurrence) is the same as affirmation, more general than being in the manner of actuality or not; so possibility is an additional modality over judgment. The point is that what is immediately understood from judgment is actuality, and possibility is a weaker degree, but that does not harm the generality, just as existence is general encompassing external and mental existence, even though what is immediately understood is external existence, and mental existence is weaker than external existence as established among philosophers. (Isma‘il)
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage defines the general possible proposition (al-mumkina al-‘āmma). It begins by explaining the phrase 'or by the absence of necessity of its contradictory'. In a proposition, if the opposite (contradictory) of the stated relation is not necessary, then the proposition is called 'possible' in the general sense. For example, 'Zayd is a writer with general possibility' means that writing is not impossible for Zayd; i.e., it is not necessary that he is not a writer. This proposition is called 'possible' because it includes imkān, which is the negation of necessity. The commentator then critiques an interpretation of 'by actuality' (bi’l-fi‘l) as meaning 'in one of the three times', arguing that this would exclude propositions whose subject is timeless (like 'The Necessary Existent is eternal'), yet such propositions are still absolute general. Instead, 'by actuality' should be understood as 'in general' (fī al-jumla), meaning actual as opposed to potential. The passage then discusses the scope: the general possible is more general than the existential non-perpetual and non-necessary. It clarifies that in an affirmative proposition, possibility means its negation is not necessary; in a negative, it means its affirmation is not necessary. Finally, it addresses a controversy: some scholars deny that the general possible is a genuine proposition because it lacks a definite judgment of occurrence or non-occurrence. However, this is refuted by noting that judgment (ḥukm) is broader than actual occurrence; possibility is an additional modality over the judgment, and a weaker degree does not negate generality, analogous to how mental existence is included under existence despite being weaker than external existence.

B) The opposite of the relation is not necessary.
C) The relation is necessary.
D) The relation is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The general possible proposition tells us that the contradictory is not necessary, not that the relation itself holds.

Is the General Possible Proposition a Genuine Proposition?
Debate on whether possible proposition is real

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection that the general possible proposition is not a real proposition and evaluate the refutation based on the broader definition of judgment (ḥukm).

Core Idea
Judgment (ḥukm) is broader than actual occurrence; possibility is an additional modality.

Some logicians argued that the general possible proposition cannot be a proposition because it does not include a definite judgment of occurrence or non-occurrence. However, the commentator refutes this by noting that judgment encompasses modal qualifications. This debate reveals the nature of propositional judgment in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Some scholars claim: 'The general possible proposition is not a real proposition because a proposition requires a judgment (ḥukm) of occurrence or non-occurrence. The general possible lacks that.'
2
Identify the underlying assumption
The objectors assume that judgment (ḥukm) means actual occurrence (wuqū‘ fi‘lī). They equate 'judgment' with a definite statement of what is the case.
3
Analyze the refutation
The refutation expands the meaning of judgment: judgment is simply affirmation (ithbāt) or negation (nafy), and it can be modal. The general possible affirms the non-necessity of the opposite, which is a genuine judgment.
The commentator uses the example of existence: mental existence is weaker but still included under existence.
4
Apply the analogy
Just as existence includes both external and mental existence (with mental being weaker), judgment includes both actual and modal judgments (with modal being weaker). Weaker does not mean non-existent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله أو بعدم ضرورة خلافها: أي إذا حكم في القضية بأن خلاف النسبة المذكورة فيها ليس ضروريا نحو قولنا زيد كاتب بالإمكان العام يعني أن الكتابة غير مستحيلة له بمعنى أن سلبها عنه ليس بضروري سميت القضية حينئذ ممكنة؛ لاشتمالها على الإمكان وهو سلب الضرورة، وعامة؛ كما أنه بالفع أي في أحد الأزمنة الثلاثة. فيه نظر: فإن تفسير "بالفعل" بأحد الأزمنة الثلاثة باطل؛ فإنه يخرج من تعريف المطلقة العامة التي يكون موضوعها بريئاً من الزمان، كقولنا: الواجب قديم وغير ذلك مع أنها داخلة تحتها على لا يبقى حينئذ فرق بينها وبين المنتشرة والمطلقة كما لا يخفى بل المراد بالفعل ما يقابل القوة فالصواب أن يقال بدل قوله : أي في أحد الأزمنة الثلاثة "أي في الجملة" كما يظهر من كلام المصنف أيضاً في بعض تصانيفه.(إسماعيل) هذا أي كون النسبة متحققة بالفعل المفهوم من القضية فلما كان هذا المعنى مفهوم القضية المطلقة سميت بها. أعم من الوجودية إلخ لأن الأولى : عبارة عن المطلقة العامة المقيدة باللادوام الذاتي والثانية: باللاضرورة الذاتية خلاف النسبة المذكورة: فإن كانت القضية إيجابية فخلافها سلبية، فالإمكان في الموجبة بمعنى سيجيء. أن سلبها ليس ضروريا، وإن كانت سالبة فخلافها إيجابية، فالإمكان في السالبة بمعنى أن إيجابها ليس ضروريا. (برهان :ممكنة ومحمولها أيضاً عرض مفارق؛ لأنها أعم من الفعلية والفعلية أعم القضايا وأعم الأعم أعم؛ لأنها أعم من سائر القضايا فتحقق في مواد سائر القضايا ولا عكس. ممكنة ذهب بعضهم إلى أن الممكنة العامة ليست قضية حقيقة؛ فإن القضية لابد فيها من الحكم أي الوقوع واللاوقوع، ولا شك أنه غير مشتملة عليه، وإذا لم تكن قضية فكيف تكون موجبة؛ فإن الموجبة قسم من القضية وقد نقضه بعضهم بأن ذلك خطأ؛ لأن الحكم أي الوقوع عبارة عن نفس الثبوت أعم من أن يكون على نهج الفعلية أو لا، فالإمكان كيفية زائدة على الحكم غاية الأمر أن المتبادر من الحكم الفعلية، والإمكان أضعف المدارج لكنه لا يضر في العمومية كما أن الوجود أعم شامل للموجود الخارجي والذهني مع أن المتبادر هو الوجود الخارجي، والوجود الذهني أضعف من الخارجي كما تقرر عند الحكماء. إسماعيل)
His saying: 'or by the absence of necessity of its contradictory' means: if it is judged in the proposition that the opposite of the mentioned relation is not necessary, like our saying: 'Zayd is a writer with general possibility', meaning that writing is not impossible for him, i.e., its negation is not necessary. Then the proposition is called 'possible' because it includes possibility, which is the negation of necessity; and 'general'; as it is by actuality i.e., in one of the three times. There is a consideration: for interpreting 'by actuality' as one of the three times is invalid; because it would exclude from the definition of absolute general propositions whose subject is free from time, like our saying: 'The Necessary Existent is eternal' and others, even though they are included under it, and then there would be no difference between it and the widespread and absolute as is clear. But the intended meaning of 'by actuality' is what is opposite to potentiality, so the correct thing is to say instead of his saying 'i.e., in one of the three times' 'i.e., in general', as appears from the author's words also in some of his works. (Isma‘il) This, i.e., that the relation is actualized, is what is understood from the proposition. Since this meaning is the meaning of the absolute proposition, it is named by it. More general than the existential etc., because the first is the absolute general restricted by the lack of essential perpetuity and the second by the lack of essential necessity. The opposite of the mentioned relation: if the proposition is affirmative, its opposite is negative, so possibility in the affirmative means that its negation is not necessary; if it is negative, its opposite is affirmative, so possibility in the negative means that its affirmation is not necessary. (Burhan) Possible: and its predicate is also a separable accident because it is more general than the actual, and the actual is the most general of propositions, and the most general of the most general is the most general because it is more general than other propositions, so it is realized in the matters of other propositions and not vice versa. Possible: some went to the view that the general possible is not a real proposition; for a proposition necessarily includes judgment, i.e., occurrence or non-occurrence, and there is no doubt that it does not include it. If it is not a proposition, how can it be affirmative? For the affirmative is a division of the proposition. Some refuted this by saying that is an error; because judgment (i.e., occurrence) is the same as affirmation, more general than being in the manner of actuality or not; so possibility is an additional modality over judgment. The point is that what is immediately understood from judgment is actuality, and possibility is a weaker degree, but that does not harm the generality, just as existence is general encompassing external and mental existence, even though what is immediately understood is external existence, and mental existence is weaker than external existence as established among philosophers. (Isma‘il)

Commentary
This passage defines the general possible proposition (al-mumkina al-‘āmma). It begins by explaining the phrase 'or by the absence of necessity of its contradictory'. In a proposition, if the opposite (contradictory) of the stated relation is not necessary, then the proposition is called 'possible' in the general sense. For example, 'Zayd is a writer with general possibility' means that writing is not impossible for Zayd; i.e., it is not necessary that he is not a writer. This proposition is called 'possible' because it includes imkān, which is the negation of necessity. The commentator then critiques an interpretation of 'by actuality' (bi’l-fi‘l) as meaning 'in one of the three times', arguing that this would exclude propositions whose subject is timeless (like 'The Necessary Existent is eternal'), yet such propositions are still absolute general. Instead, 'by actuality' should be understood as 'in general' (fī al-jumla), meaning actual as opposed to potential. The passage then discusses the scope: the general possible is more general than the existential non-perpetual and non-necessary. It clarifies that in an affirmative proposition, possibility means its negation is not necessary; in a negative, it means its affirmation is not necessary. Finally, it addresses a controversy: some scholars deny that the general possible is a genuine proposition because it lacks a definite judgment of occurrence or non-occurrence. However, this is refuted by noting that judgment (ḥukm) is broader than actual occurrence; possibility is an additional modality over the judgment, and a weaker degree does not negate generality, analogous to how mental existence is included under existence despite being weaker than external existence.

The Eight Simple Modal Propositions
Author lists the eight simple modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the eight simple modal propositions and determine whether a given modal proposition is simple based on its reality.

Core Idea
The eight simples are the foundational modal types; each is purely affirmative or purely negative.

In this passage, the author reaffirms that the eight modal propositions previously listed are all simple. Understanding these eight is essential for analyzing modal judgments in Islamic logic, especially in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the eight simples
Find the list in the Arabic: الضرورية المطلقة، الدائمة المطلقة، المشروطة العامة، العرفية العامة، الوقتية المطلقة، المنتشرة المطلقة، المطلقة العامة، الممكنة العامة.
These are the same eight discussed earlier.
2
Understand 'simple' definition
A simple modal proposition is one whose reality (حقيقة) is either pure affirmation or pure negation, not a mix. The eight listed each have only one judgment.
Simplicity here is about the reality, not wording.
3
Test with examples
Take a proposition like 'Every human is necessarily an animal' (ضرورية). It is affirmative only. 'Some human is not necessarily a writer' is negative only. Both are simple.
Check each of the eight against this criterion.
4
Recognize the exception of general possible
The general possible (الممكنة العامة) is considered simple but later discussion shows it might involve two judgments. The author resolves this by convention.
This will be explored more in the next lesson.

فهذه بسائط: أي القضايا الثمانية المذكورة من جملة الموجبات بسائط. اعلم أن القضية الموجهة إما بسيطة وهي ما تكون حقيقتها إما إيجابا فقط أو سلبا فقط كما مر من الموجهات الثمانية، وإما مركبة وهي التي تكون حقيقتها مركبة من إيجاب وسلب بشرط أن لا يكون الجزء الثاني فيها مذكورا بعبارة مستقلة سواء كان في اللفظ تركيب، كقولنا كل إنسان ضاحك بالفعل لا دائما، فقولنا: "لا دائما" إشارة إلى حكم سلبي أي لا شيء من الإنسان بضاحك بالفعل، أو لم يكن في اللفظ تركيب كقولنا كل إنسان كاتب بالإمكان الخاص؛ فإنه في المعنى قضيتان ممكنتان عامتان أي كل إنسان كاتب بالإمكان العام، ولا شيء من الإنسان بكاتب بالإمكان العام والعبرة في الإيجاب والسلب حينئذ الممكنة الخاصة: فإن الحكم فيها بسلب الضرورة من كلا الطرفين، فكأنها مركبة من الممكنتين العامتين كما ستعلم. إسماعيل) القضايا الثمانية: ضرورية مطلقة ودائمة مطلقة ومشروطة عامة وعرفية عامة ووقتية مطلقة ومنتشرة مطلقة ومطلقة عامة وممكنة عامة في اللفظ أي في اللفظ الدال على ذلك الجزء تركيب بأن لا يدل بحسب اللغة على سلب النسبة المذكورة بل بحسب اصطلاحهم؛ فإن لفظ الإمكان الخاص بحسب اللغة لا يدل على سلب النسبة المذكورة بالإمكان العام بل بحسب الاصطلاح. (شوستري) والعبرة في الإيجاب: دفع لما استشكله المعلم الثاني من أن حقيقة القضية المركبة لما كانت مركبة من الإيجاب والسلب، فكانت كالخنثى المشكل فهي ليست بموجبة ولا سالبة، فانحصار القضية فيهما باطل. (عبد)
"So these are simple": i.e., the eight mentioned propositions from among the affirmatives are simple. Know that the modal proposition is either simple, which is what its reality is either affirmation only or negation only as passed from the eight modal propositions, or compound, which is what its reality is composed of affirmation and negation on condition that the second part in it is not mentioned in an independent expression, whether there is composition in the wording, like our saying: Every human is laughing by actuality, not perpetually. So our saying: 'not perpetually' indicates a negative judgment, i.e., no human is laughing by actuality. Or there is no composition in the wording, like our saying: Every human is a writer with special possibility; for it meaningfully is two general possible propositions, i.e., every human is a writer with general possibility, and no human is a writer with general possibility. And the criterion in affirmation and negation then is the special possible; for the judgment in it is the negation of necessity from both sides, so it is as if it is composed of the two general possibles as you will come to know. (Ismail) The eight propositions: absolute necessary, absolute perpetual, general conditional, general customary, absolute temporal, absolute distributed, general absolute, and general possible. In the wording, i.e., in the wording indicating that part [is] composition, such that it does not indicate by language the negation of the mentioned relation but by their convention; for the expression 'special possible' by language does not indicate the negation of the mentioned relation with general possibility but by convention. (Abd) Two general possible propositions: for special possibility is the negation of necessity from both sides. So considering the negation of necessity from the side of affirmation, a negative general possible proposition results; and considering the negation of necessity from the side of negation, an affirmative general possible proposition results. (Ismail: two general possible propositions) And here there is a research: that the judgment of simplicity in other than the general possible is apparent with no concealment, but as for the general possible there is obscurity. For if we say the general possible includes judgment in the agreeing side, it follows that on this appreciation it includes two different judgments, so how can it be simple? And if we say that it does not include judgment in the agreeing side, as is apparent from their expressions and what is mentioned in Sharh al-Matali‘, it follows that the possible is not a proposition on this appreciation. So what is the way in making it simple? Unless one resorts to metaphor. (Shustari) And the criterion in affirmation: a push for what the Second Teacher objected, that the reality of the compound proposition, since it is composed of affirmation and negation, is like a hermaphrodite, it is neither affirmative nor negative, so the restriction of the proposition to these two is false. (Abd)
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage classifies modal propositions into simple (basīṭa) and compound (murakkaba). The simple ones are the eight listed: absolute necessary, absolute perpetual, general conditional, general customary, absolute temporal, absolute distributed, general absolute, and general possible. Each is purely affirmative or purely negative. A compound proposition is one whose meaning combines affirmation and negation, even if the wording does not explicitly state both parts. Two examples are given: 'Every human is laughing actually, not perpetually' contains a negative part ('not perpetually'), so it is compound; 'Every human is a writer with special possibility' is compound in meaning because special possibility implies both a general affirmative possible and a general negative possible. The passage then discusses an issue: the general possible seems problematic for simplicity because it might imply two judgments. Shustari questions whether it can be simple given that it either contains two judgments or no judgment. The response by Abd defends the classification by noting that the criterion for affirmation/negation in compound propositions is conventional, and the objection of the Second Teacher (al-Farabi) that compound propositions are neither affirmative nor negative is rebutted: they are considered affirmative or negative based on the predominant part.

مشروطة عامة
عرفية عامة
وقتية مطلقة
منتشرة مطلقة
مطلقة عامة
ممكنة عامة

Simple vs. Compound Modal Propositions
Author distinguishes simple from compound modal propositions and discusses the problematic case of the general possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a simple modal proposition from a compound one and explain why the general possible is treated as simple by convention.

Core Idea
Compound propositions combine affirmation and negation in meaning, even if wording is simple; special possibility is the key example.

This passage delves into the distinction between simple and compound modal propositions. A compound proposition, though it may look simple in wording, actually contains both an affirmation and a negation. The special possible (الإمكان الخاص) is a prime example, as it implies two general possible judgments. The authors also address an objection by al-Farabi that compound propositions should not be considered propositions at all.

Guided Path
1
Define compound proposition
A compound modal proposition is one whose reality is composed of affirmation and negation, but the second part is not stated as an independent clause. It can have compound wording (e.g., 'every human is laughing actually, not perpetually') or simple wording (e.g., 'every human is a writer with special possibility').
The key is the meaning, not the words.
2
Analyze the 'special possible' example
The proposition 'Every human is a writer with special possibility' means two things: 'Every human is possibly a writer' (general possible affirmative) and 'No human is necessarily a writer' (general possible negative). So it is compound in meaning.
Special possibility negates necessity from both sides.
3
Understand the problem with general possible
The general possible (الممكنة العامة) appears to be simple but might imply two judgments. The commentators debate this: if it includes judgment on the agreeing side, it has two different judgments; if not, it is not a proposition. The resolution is that it is considered simple by convention (تجوز).
This shows the complexity of classification.
4
Address al-Farabi's objection
Al-Farabi objected that compound propositions are neither purely affirmative nor purely negative, so the division of propositions into affirmative and negative is incomplete. The author rebuts that the criterion for affirmation/negation in compounds is based on the predominant part or convention.
This defends the traditional binary classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذه بسائط: أي القضايا الثمانية المذكورة من جملة الموجبات بسائط. اعلم أن القضية الموجهة إما بسيطة وهي ما تكون حقيقتها إما إيجابا فقط أو سلبا فقط كما مر من الموجهات الثمانية، وإما مركبة وهي التي تكون حقيقتها مركبة من إيجاب وسلب بشرط أن لا يكون الجزء الثاني فيها مذكورا بعبارة مستقلة سواء كان في اللفظ تركيب، كقولنا كل إنسان ضاحك بالفعل لا دائما، فقولنا: "لا دائما" إشارة إلى حكم سلبي أي لا شيء من الإنسان بضاحك بالفعل، أو لم يكن في اللفظ تركيب كقولنا كل إنسان كاتب بالإمكان الخاص؛ فإنه في المعنى قضيتان ممكنتان عامتان أي كل إنسان كاتب بالإمكان العام، ولا شيء من الإنسان بكاتب بالإمكان العام والعبرة في الإيجاب والسلب حينئذ الممكنة الخاصة: فإن الحكم فيها بسلب الضرورة من كلا الطرفين، فكأنها مركبة من الممكنتين العامتين كما ستعلم. إسماعيل) القضايا الثمانية: ضرورية مطلقة ودائمة مطلقة ومشروطة عامة وعرفية عامة ووقتية مطلقة ومنتشرة مطلقة ومطلقة عامة وممكنة عامة في اللفظ أي في اللفظ الدال على ذلك الجزء تركيب بأن لا يدل بحسب اللغة على سلب النسبة المذكورة بل بحسب اصطلاحهم؛ فإن لفظ الإمكان الخاص بحسب اللغة لا يدل على سلب النسبة المذكورة بالإمكان العام بل بحسب الاصطلاح. (عبد) قضيتان ممكنتان عامتان فإن الإمكان الخاص عبارة عن سلب الضرورة من الجانبين، فباعتبار سلب الضرورة عن جانب الإيجاب يحصل قضية سالبة ممكنة عامة وباعتبار سلب الضرورة عن جانب السلب يحصل موجبة ممكنة عامة (إسماعيل ممكنتان عامتان وههنا بحث وهو أن الحكم بالبساطة في غير الممكنة العامة ظاهر لا سترة فيه، وأما الممكنة العامة ففيها خفاء؛ إذ لو قلنا الممكنة العامة مشتملة على الحكم في الجانب الموافق اتجه أنها على هذا التقدير مشتملة على حكمين مختلفين فكيف تكون بسيطة؟ وإن قلنا إنها لم تكن مشتملة على الحكم في الجانب الموافق كما هو الظاهر من عباراتهم وهو المذكور في شرح المطالع اتجه أن المكنة لم تكن قضية على هذا التقدير فما الوجه في جعلها بسيطة؟ اللهم إلا أن يتمسك بالتجوز. (شوستري) والعبرة في الإيجاب: دفع لما استشكله المعلم الثاني من أن حقيقة القضية المركبة لما كانت مركبة من الإيجاب والسلب، فكانت كالخنثى المشكل فهي ليست بموجبة ولا سالبة، فانحصار القضية فيهما باطل. (عبد)
"So these are simple": i.e., the eight mentioned propositions from among the affirmatives are simple. Know that the modal proposition is either simple, which is what its reality is either affirmation only or negation only as passed from the eight modal propositions, or compound, which is what its reality is composed of affirmation and negation on condition that the second part in it is not mentioned in an independent expression, whether there is composition in the wording, like our saying: Every human is laughing by actuality, not perpetually. So our saying: 'not perpetually' indicates a negative judgment, i.e., no human is laughing by actuality. Or there is no composition in the wording, like our saying: Every human is a writer with special possibility; for it meaningfully is two general possible propositions, i.e., every human is a writer with general possibility, and no human is a writer with general possibility. And the criterion in affirmation and negation then is the special possible; for the judgment in it is the negation of necessity from both sides, so it is as if it is composed of the two general possibles as you will come to know. (Ismail) The eight propositions: absolute necessary, absolute perpetual, general conditional, general customary, absolute temporal, absolute distributed, general absolute, and general possible. In the wording, i.e., in the wording indicating that part [is] composition, such that it does not indicate by language the negation of the mentioned relation but by their convention; for the expression 'special possible' by language does not indicate the negation of the mentioned relation with general possibility but by convention. (Abd) Two general possible propositions: for special possibility is the negation of necessity from both sides. So considering the negation of necessity from the side of affirmation, a negative general possible proposition results; and considering the negation of necessity from the side of negation, an affirmative general possible proposition results. (Ismail: two general possible propositions) And here there is a research: that the judgment of simplicity in other than the general possible is apparent with no concealment, but as for the general possible there is obscurity. For if we say the general possible includes judgment in the agreeing side, it follows that on this appreciation it includes two different judgments, so how can it be simple? And if we say that it does not include judgment in the agreeing side, as is apparent from their expressions and what is mentioned in Sharh al-Matali‘, it follows that the possible is not a proposition on this appreciation. So what is the way in making it simple? Unless one resorts to metaphor. (Shustari) And the criterion in affirmation: a push for what the Second Teacher objected, that the reality of the compound proposition, since it is composed of affirmation and negation, is like a hermaphrodite, it is neither affirmative nor negative, so the restriction of the proposition to these two is false. (Abd)
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage classifies modal propositions into simple (basīṭa) and compound (murakkaba). The simple ones are the eight listed: absolute necessary, absolute perpetual, general conditional, general customary, absolute temporal, absolute distributed, general absolute, and general possible. Each is purely affirmative or purely negative. A compound proposition is one whose meaning combines affirmation and negation, even if the wording does not explicitly state both parts. Two examples are given: 'Every human is laughing actually, not perpetually' contains a negative part ('not perpetually'), so it is compound; 'Every human is a writer with special possibility' is compound in meaning because special possibility implies both a general affirmative possible and a general negative possible. The passage then discusses an issue: the general possible seems problematic for simplicity because it might imply two judgments. Shustari questions whether it can be simple given that it either contains two judgments or no judgment. The response by Abd defends the classification by noting that the criterion for affirmation/negation in compound propositions is conventional, and the objection of the Second Teacher (al-Farabi) that compound propositions are neither affirmative nor negative is rebutted: they are considered affirmative or negative based on the predominant part.

Key Terms
مركبة
الإمكان الخاص
ممكنة عامة
المعلم الثاني (الفارابي)

D) Because it is expressed in a complex sentence.

Key Takeaway
A compound modal proposition combines affirmation and negation in its meaning, and special possibility is the clearest example of how a single expression can imply two judgments.

Simple and Composite Modal Propositions Defined
Author defines simple and composite propositions with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish simple from composite modal propositions using the criteria of affirmation/negation and independence of the second part.

Core Idea
A simple proposition says one thing; a composite says two but not as two separate sentences.

In this passage, the author defines two types of modal propositions: simple and composite. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing modal syllogisms in Islamic logic. The examples show how a proposition can mix affirmation and negation without being two separate statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining feature of a simple proposition
The author says a simple proposition's reality is either only affirmation or only negation. Look at the eight modals mentioned earlier: they are all simple because each expresses one modal judgment.
The eight modals include necessary, impossible, possible, etc.
2
Identify the defining feature of a composite proposition
A composite proposition's reality is composed of affirmation and negation, but the negative part is not stated as an independent sentence. It can be explicit in the wording (like 'not always') or only in meaning (like 'with specific possibility').
The condition is crucial: if the second part were an independent statement, it would be two propositions, not one composite.
3
Examine the first example: 'Every human is laughing actually, not always'
Here 'not always' indicates a negative judgment (no human is laughing always). The proposition is still considered one composite because the negative part is not an independent clause.
The word 'not' modifies the modality, not the whole proposition.
4
Examine the second example: 'Every human is a writer with specific possibility'
Specific possibility means negation of necessity from both sides. In meaning, this equates to two general possible propositions: 'Every human is a writer with general possibility' and 'No human is a writer with general possibility'. But since the second is not stated independently, it remains one composite proposition.
The composition is in the meaning, not in the wording.

Commentary
This passage defines two types of modal propositions: simple and composite. A simple proposition is purely affirmative or purely negative, exemplified by the eight modals. A composite proposition mixes affirmation and negation, but the second part is not an independent statement. This discussion is crucial for understanding how modal propositions are classified in Islamic logic, especially in distinguishing propositions used in syllogistic reasoning.

The General Possible: Simple or Composite?
Author investigates the simplicity of the general possible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the puzzle regarding whether the general possible proposition is simple or composite, and evaluate the proposed metaphorical solution.

Core Idea
The general possible seems to include two judgments, but that would make it composite; yet if it only has one, it might not be a proposition at all.

The author presents a subtle problem: the general possible proposition ('it is possible that S is P') seems to involve a judgment that something is not impossible. This could be interpreted as having both an affirmative and negative side. The author grapples with whether such a proposition can still be considered simple.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem: the general possible proposition
The general possible is defined as 'what is not impossible'. This seems to involve two aspects: affirming possibility and negating impossibility. The author asks: if it includes judgment on both sides, it would be composite; if it includes only one side, it might not be a genuine proposition.
The eight modals are all simple except possibly the general possible.
2
Consider the first horn of the dilemma
If the general possible includes a judgment on the affirmative side (i.e., it affirms possibility), then it seems to have both affirmation and negation, making it composite. But the author says simplicity is apparent for other modals.
The author says 'if we say the general possible includes judgment in the affirmative side' then it would have two judgments.
3
Consider the second horn of the dilemma
If the general possible does not include a judgment on the affirmative side, then it is just the negation of impossibility. But a pure negation without an affirmative judgment might not be considered a full proposition. The author references Sharh al-Mutali' for this view.
This would make the general possible not a complete proposition.
4
Evaluate the proposed solution: metaphorical interpretation
To preserve its simplicity, the author suggests resorting to a metaphorical interpretation. This means taking the proposition in a non-literal sense to avoid the contradiction. The exact nature of this metaphor is left for advanced discussion.
This is a typical move in Islamic logic when a definitional problem arises.

Understanding Self-Non-Permanence (al-La Dawam al-Dhati)
Author defines and clarifies self-non-permanence and its application.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define self-non-permanence and identify its application in temporal and general propositions from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Self-non-permanence means a proposition's relation is not permanent throughout the subject's existence.

In Islamic logic, modal propositions are often qualified with conditions. One crucial condition is self-non-permanence (al-la dawam al-dhati), which applies to temporal and general propositions. This lesson clarifies its meaning and how it is used to distinguish temporary from permanent judgments, which is essential for fiqh rulings that are time-bound.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate 'al-la dawam al-dhati' in the Arabic passage. Note that it qualifies both the two general and the two temporal propositions.
2
Extract the definition of self-non-permanence
Find the sentence: 'the relation mentioned in the proposition is not permanent as long as the subject's essence exists.' This is the precise definition; write it down.
3
Apply the definition to an example
Consider a temporal proposition: 'Zayd is standing at noon.' The relation 'standing' holds only at noon, not as long as Zayd exists. This fits self-non-permanence.
Use an Islamic example: 'The prayer is obligatory at its time.' The obligation is not permanent throughout the person's life.
4
Distinguish from other modalities
Understand that self-non-permanence differs from descriptive non-permanence (al-la dawam al-wasfi). The passage mentions that descriptive non-permanence contradicts the descriptive necessity considered in the specific conditioned proposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد تقيد العامتان والوقتيتان المطلقتان باللادوام الذاتي. قوله العامتان أي المشروطة العامة والعرفية العامة. قوله: والوقتيتان: أي الوقتية المطلقة والمنتشرة المطلقة. قوله: باللادوام الذاتي: ومعنى اللادوام الذاتي أن النسبة المذكورة في القضية ليست دائمة ما دام ذات الموضوع موجودة. بالجزء الأول: إن قيل: إطلاق الموجبة والسالبة على القضية المركبة باعتبار الجزء الأول إما على سبيل الحقيقة أو على طريق المجاز، لا سبيل إلى الثاني؛ فإن قسمة القضية إلى الموجبة والسالبة حصر عقلي، فلابد أن يكون كل قضية داخلة في أحد القسمين على الحقيقة ولا سبيل إلى الأول أيضاً؛ لأن كون القضية المركبة داخلة في الموجبة أو السالبة على سبيل الحقيقة ظاهر البطلان؛ لاستحالة دخول المركب من قسمين في أحدهما. فالجواب: أن القضية المقيدة باللادوام واللاضرورة قضية واحدة في الحقيقة لعدم التركيب في اللفظ بين القضيتين، فإن كانت موجبة فموجبة وإن سالبة فسالبة. أما سمعت أن المقيد مع قيده أمر واحد ولهذا قالوا: لا عبرة له، فالحال أن القضية المركبة موجهة بجهة اللادوام أو اللاضرورة التي يفهم منها قضية أخرى بالالتزام على المذهب الصحيح، فلا تركيب هناك من قضيتين حقيقة. فافهم. (ثم قال) أي الوقتية المطلقة إلخ: إنما قال لهما: الوقتيتان دون المطلقتين؛ لأن الوقت معتبرة في كليهما في الأول على سبيل التعيين وفي الثانية على سبيل الانتشار، فهما وقتيتان بلا شبهة بخلاف ما إذا قال: مطلقتين؛ فإنه لعله يذهب الوهم إلى أن المراد الضرورية المطلقة والدائمة المطلقة على أنه لا يصح تقييدهما باللادوام الذاتي كما سيجيء. (ثم قال) باللا دوام الذاتي: إنما اعتبروا في مفهوم المشروطة الخاصة تقييد الحكم باللادوام الذاتي؛ لأنه المعتبر في مفهومه اصطلاحا. وأما تقييده باللادوام الوصفي واللاضرورة الوصفية فغير صحيح قطعا؛ لمنافاتها الضرورة الوصفية المعتبرة في عامها وأما تقييده بقيود أخرى وإن كان صحيحاً كاللا ضرورة الأزلية والذاتية أو غيرهما فلم يعتبر فيه اصطلاحا وقس عليه نظائرها.
And the two general and the two absolute temporal (propositions) have been qualified by self-non-permanence. His saying 'the two general' means the general conditioned and the general conventional. His saying 'and the two temporal' means the absolute temporal and the absolute distributed. His saying 'by self-non-permanence' and the meaning of self-non-permanence is that the relation mentioned in the proposition is not permanent as long as the subject's essence exists. 'By the first part': If it is said: applying the terms affirmative and negative to the composite proposition based on the first part is either in truth or metaphorically; the second way is not possible because the division of proposition into affirmative and negative is a rational division, so every proposition must fall under one of the two divisions in truth. And the first way is also not possible because the composite proposition being included in the affirmative or negative in truth is clearly false, as it is impossible for a composite of two parts to be included in one of them. The answer: that the proposition qualified by non-permanence and non-necessity is really one proposition because there is no composition in wording between the two propositions; so if it is affirmative, it is affirmative, and if negative, it is negative. Have you not heard that the qualified together with its qualification is one thing, and therefore they said: there is no consideration for it. Thus, the composite proposition is modalized with the modality of non-permanence or non-necessity, from which another proposition is understood by implication according to the correct school, so there is no composition of two propositions in reality. So understand. (Then he said:) 'The absolute temporal etc.' He only called them the two temporal and not the two absolute, because time is considered in both: in the first as specific, in the second as distributed, so they are undoubtedly temporal, unlike if he said 'the two absolute', for maybe the mind would go to mean the absolute necessary and the absolute permanent, which cannot be qualified by self-non-permanence as will come. (Then he said:) 'By self-non-permanence' They only considered in the concept of the specific conditioned the qualification of the judgment by self-non-permanence because it is the considered in its concept terminologically. As for its qualification by descriptive non-permanence and descriptive non-necessity, it is incorrect absolutely because they contradict the descriptive necessity considered in its general. As for its qualification by other restrictions, even if correct like eternal non-necessity and essential non-necessity or others, it was not considered terminologically, and analogize to it its parallels.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage explains the qualification of specific modal propositions — the two general (conditioned general and conventional general) and the two absolute temporal (absolute temporal and distributed temporal) — with the condition of 'self-non-permanence' (al-la dawam al-dhati). This means that the relation (e.g., being laughing) does not hold permanently as long as the subject's essence exists; it only holds at some time. The author then addresses an objection: how can a composite proposition be classified as either affirmative or negative, given that it contains both affirmation and negation? The objection argues that it cannot be by metaphor (since division is rational) nor by truth (since a composite cannot be one of its parts). The answer is that such a composite proposition is actually one proposition in reality because the modality (like non-permanence) changes the nature: the proposition is either affirmative or negative based on the first part, and the modality implies another proposition but does not create a real composition in wording. The author then clarifies why the temporal propositions are called 'temporal' rather than 'absolute' to avoid confusion with necessary/permanent propositions. Finally, he specifies that only self-non-permanence (and not descriptive non-permanence) is the proper qualification for the specific conditioned proposition, as defined in the convention. This is a typical expansion by a commentator to clarify the technical nuances of modal propositions in Islamic logic, relevant to fiqh reasoning about temporal rulings.

عرفية عامة
وقتية مطلقة

C) The proposition is false at all times.

Self-non-permanence is a key modal condition indicating that a proposition's predicate holds only temporarily relative to the subject's existence.

Resolving the Composite Proposition Objection
Author responds to an objection about classifying composite propositions as affirmative or negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a composite modal proposition is considered a single proposition in Islamic logic and defend its classification as affirmative or negative.

Core Idea
A composite proposition with modality is one proposition, not two; the modality does not create a real composition.

An objector might argue that if a proposition implies another, it cannot be simply called affirmative or negative—it seems composite. The commentator provides a clear answer: the qualified proposition is a single unit in reality because the qualification is part of the expression. The implied proposition is understood by implication but does not form a separate proposition in wording. This resolution preserves the rational division of propositions into affirmative and negative.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
The objection claims that applying 'affirmative' or 'negative' to a composite proposition (one that implies another) is impossible either in truth or metaphor. In truth, because a composite cannot be one of its parts; metaphorically, because the division is rational and must be in truth.
2
Identify the two possibilities refuted
The objector considers two options: (a) the proposition is called affirmative/negative in truth, or (b) metaphorically. Both are rejected: (b) because the division is rational and requires truth; (a) because a composite cannot be one part.
3
Follow the answer given
The answer states that the proposition qualified by non-permanence or non-necessity is really one proposition. There is no composition in wording because the qualification is not a separate proposition but a modifier.
4
Understand the key principle
The commentator says: 'the qualified together with its qualification is one thing.' The modality (e.g., non-permanence) is part of the proposition, not an additional proposition. So the proposition is either affirmative or negative based on the first part.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider: 'Every prayer is obligatory as long as the person is accountable.' This implies another proposition ('Every prayer is obligatory at some times'), but the whole is one proposition: affirmative. The modality 'as long as' does not make it two propositions.
Use a fiqh example: 'Fasting in Ramadan is obligatory as long as one is healthy' is a single conditional proposition.
