Commentary
This passage is a section on composite modal propositions. The author first identifies two specific composites: al-wujūdiyya al-lā dā'ima (the existence non-permanent) and al-mumkina al-khāṣṣa (the special possible). He explains that al-wujūdiyya al-lā dā'ima is composed of two general absolute propositions, one affirmative and one negative. For example, 'No human is actually breathing non-permanently' actually means 'Every human is actually breathing,' which combines an affirmative and a negative general absolute. Then he discusses al-mumkina al-khāṣṣa, which is formed by adding no-necessity from the agreeing side as well, resulting in a proposition that affirms possibility on both sides. He gives the example 'Every human is a writer with special possibility,' which means 'Every human is a writer with general possibility' and 'No human is a writer with general possibility.' The author then lists the seven well-known composite modals: al-mashrūṭa al-khāṣṣa, al-'urfiyya al-khāṣṣa, al-waqtiyya, al-muntashira, al-wujūdiyya al-lā ḍarūriyya, al-wujūdiyya al-lā dā'ima, and al-mumkina al-khāṣṣa. He notes that many other combinations are possible because the modes are not limited to necessity, permanence, no-necessity, and non-permanence. Permanence is divided into three types, and possibility (no-necessity) has four meanings. The commentator ʿAbd adds that anyone who understands the nature of jihāt can derive any number of modals. Finally, the passage concludes with a technical remark that the composite of no-necessity yields a general possible proposition that differs in quality but agrees in quantity with the original proposition it qualifies.

Identifying the Connected Conditional Proposition
Author defines the connected conditional (shartiyya muttasila) and distinguishes it from categorical propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a connected conditional proposition (shartiyya muttasila) and distinguish it from a categorical proposition based on the structure of conditionality.

Core Idea
A connected conditional judges one relation on condition of another – not just any two statements.

In this section, the author defines the connected conditional proposition and clarifies its essential features. The commentary by Nūr Allāh and Ismāʿīl expands on the concepts of quality and quantity, and warns against confusing conditionals with categorical propositions. Understanding this distinction is critical for correctly analyzing arguments in Islamic logic and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the core definition
The author defines the connected conditional as a proposition that affirms or denies a relation (n) on condition of another relation (taqdīr). Focus on the phrase 'on condition of another' – this is the key differentiator.
2
Analyze the condition (taqdīr)
The condition can be affirmative or negative, and the two relations may be both affirmative, both negative, or mixed. The commentary notes that the condition is not merely a temporal sequence but a logical dependency.
3
Check quality and quantity agreement
The commentators explain that in a connected conditional, both component judgments must agree in quantity (both universal or both particular) but differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). This ensures the structure is conditional, not categorical.
The original proposition (the conditional) is 'qualified by non-permanence and no-necessity' from earlier discussion.
4
Distinguish from categorical propositions
The passage warns that definitions of conditionals do not apply to categorical examples like 'Day exists on condition of the sun's rising' because the relation there is causal, not conditional. Use the example 'Whenever Zayd is not an animal, he is not a human' as a genuine connected conditional.
The concept of conditionality must be intrinsic to the proposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل الشرطية متصلة إن حكم فيها بثبوت نسبة على تقدير أخرى أو نفيها لزومية إن كان ذلك :قوله مخالفتي الكيفية : أي في الإيجاب والسلب، وقد مر بيان ذلك في بيان معنى اللادوام واللا ضرورة. وأما الموافقة في الكمية والجزئية فلأن الموضوع في القضية المركبة أمر واحد قد حكم عليه بحكمين مختلفين بالإيجاب والسلب فإن كان الحكم في الجزء الأول على كل الأفراد كان في الجزء الثاني أيضاً على كلها، وإن كان على البعض في الأول فكذا في الثاني: قوله: لما قيد بهما: أي القضية التي قيّدت بهما أي باللادوام واللاضرورة يعني أصل القضية. قوله: على تقدير أخرى سواء كانت النسبتان ثبوتيتين أو سلبيتين أو مختلفتين. فقولنا: "كلما لم يكن زيد حيوانا لم يكن إنسانا متصلة موجبة، فالمتصلة الموجبة ما حكم فيها باتصال النسبتين، · مخالفتي الكيفية إلخ : اعلم أن قوله مخالفتي "الكيفية حال عن مطلقة عامة وممكنة عامة أو صفة لهما، وقوله: : "موافقتي الكمية" حال بعد حال أو صفة بعد صفة لهما، وقوله: "لما قيد "بهما متعلق بالمخالفة والموافقة على سبيل التنازع وضمير التثنية عائد إلى اللا دوام ،واللا ضرورة، والكيفية عبارة عن الإيجاب والسلب والكمية عن الكلية والجزئية. (نور الله شوستري) وأما الموافقة إلخ كون هذه القضية موافقة للأصل في الكلية والجزئية لم يظهر من بيان معنى اللادوام، ودخوله في التفريع على تحقيق معنى اللادوام استطرادي كما مر الإيماء إليه. فافهم. إسماعيل) أي القضية التي إخ: يعني به أن المراد من "ما" الموصولة القضية التي هي ! والضمير المرفوع راجع إليه والضمير المجرور إلى اللادوام ،واللاضرورة، وقد جوز بعضهم إرجاع الضمير المجرور إلى المطلقة العامة والممكنة العامة ولا شك أنه ركيك؛ فإن التقييد إنما هو باللادوام واللاضرورة لا بالقضيتين المفهومتين منهما. (إسماعيل) على تقدير إلخ ولا يتوهم أن تعريف المتصلة صادق على مثل قولنا : النهار موجود على تقدير ثبوت الطلوع للشمس وثبوت الوجود للنهار متصل لثبوت طلوع الشمس وتعريف المنفصلة صادق على قولنا: زوجية العدد والفردية متنافيتان؛ وذلك لأن مفهوم الشرطية معتبر في مفهومات أقسامها، فخرج الحمليات. (نور الله)
Section: The conditional (sharṭiyya) is connected (muttaṣila) if judgment is made in it of the affirmation of a relation on condition of another relation, or its negation. It is necessary (luzūmiyya) if that (connection) is ... His statement: 'differing in quality' i.e., in affirmation and negation. And the explanation of that has already passed in the explanation of the meaning of non-permanence and no-necessity. As for agreement in quantity and particularity, because the subject in the composite proposition is one thing that has been judged with two different judgments, affirmative and negative. If the judgment in the first part is on all individuals, then in the second part it is also on all of them; and if it is on some in the first, then similarly in the second. His statement: 'with what it is qualified by' i.e., the proposition that is qualified by them, i.e., by non-permanence and no-necessity, meaning the original proposition. His statement: 'on condition of another' whether the two relations are affirmative or negative or different. Thus our statement: 'Whenever Zayd is not an animal, he is not a human' is a connected affirmative. So the connected affirmative is that in which the connection of the two relations is judged. (Nūr Allāh Shūstarī) 'Differing in quality, etc.': Know that his statement 'differing in quality' is a circumstantial qualifier or attribute for general absolute and general possible, and his statement 'agreeing in quantity' is a second circumstantial qualifier or attribute for them, and his statement 'with what it is qualified by' is related to the differing and agreeing on the basis of mutual claim, and the dual pronoun refers back to non-permanence and no-necessity. Quality (al-kayfiyya) denotes affirmation and negation, and quantity (al-kammiyya) denotes universality and particularity. (Nūr Allāh Shūstarī) 'As for the agreement, etc.' (Ismāʿīl) 'That is, the proposition which...' (Ismāʿīl) 'On condition, etc.' And do not imagine that the definition of the connected applies to like our saying: 'Day exists on condition of the sun's rising' and the existence of the day is connected to the sun's rising, and the definition of the disconnected (al-munfaṣila) applies to our saying: 'Evenness of number and oddness are mutually exclusive.' This is because the concept of conditionality is considered in the concepts of its divisions, so categorical propositions are excluded. (Nūr Allāh Shūstarī)
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage opens a new section on conditional propositions. The author defines the connected conditional (al-sharṭiyya al-muttaṣila) as a proposition that asserts or denies a relation on the condition of another relation. He then notes that it is called 'necessary' (luzūmiyya) if that conditional connection is necessary. The commentary by Nūr Allāh and Ismāʿīl clarifies aspects of the definition: the 'differing in quality' refers to the composite propositions discussed earlier, and 'agreeing in quantity' means that both parts have the same quantity (both universal or both particular). They also explain that the term 'condition' (taqdīr) can involve affirmative or negative relations. An example of a connected conditional is 'Whenever Zayd is not an animal, he is not a human.' The commentators warn against confusing the definition of the connected conditional with categorical examples: for instance, 'Day exists on condition of the sun's rising' is not a genuine conditional because the relation is not a conditional connection but a causal one; similarly, 'Evenness and oddness are mutually exclusive' is not a conditional proposition because it does not involve a condition. The defining feature is that the conditional relation is itself part of the meaning, as opposed to categorical propositions where the condition is external. Thus, the passage emphasizes the precise logical structure of conditional propositions and distinguishes them from mere categorical statements that contain a condition in their phrasing.

التقدير

Key Takeaway
The connected conditional is defined by judgment of one relation on condition of another, and it requires that the condition be part of the logical structure, not just a temporal or causal connection.

Definition and Classification of Connected Conditionals
Defines connected conditional and divides into necessary/accidental

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between necessary (luzumiyya) and accidental (ittifaqiyya) connected conditionals based on the presence of a causal relation.

Core Idea
A connected conditional is necessary only when the connection is causal; otherwise it is accidental.

In this passage, the author defines the connected conditional proposition and divides it into two types: necessary and accidental. The key factor is whether the connection between the antecedent and consequent is based on a causal relation (ʿalāqa) or not. This distinction is foundational for evaluating the strength of conditional arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of connected conditional
Find the core definition: a judgment affirming or denying a relation on condition of another proposition.
The passage states: 'الشرطية متصلة إن حكم فيها بثبوت نسبة على تقدير أخرى أو نفيها'.
2
Understand the role of causal relation (ʿalāqa)
Notice that the necessary type requires a causal link between antecedent and consequent; accidental lacks it.
The term 'علاقة' is the distinguishing factor.
3
Classify given examples
Use the examples: 'Sun rising and day existing' is necessary; 'Human speaking and donkey braying' is accidental.
The passage provides both affirmative and negative examples.
4
Handle negatives
Understand that negation of a necessary conditional can be either no connection at all or connection without cause.
The author clarifies this in the second half of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشرطية متصلة إن حكم فيها بثبوت نسبة على تقدير أخرى أو نفيها لزومية إن كان ذلك بعلاقة وإلا فاتفاقية. والسالبة ما حكم فيها بسلب اتصالهما نحو ليس البتة كلما كانت الشمس طالعة كان الليل موجودا. واللزومية الموجبة ما حكم فيها بالاتصال بعلاقة والسالبة ما حكم فيها بأنه ليس هناك اتصال بعلاقة سواء لم يكن هناك اتصال أو كان لكن لا بعلاقة. وأما الاتفاقية فهي ما حكم فيها بمجرد الاتصال أو نفيه من غير أن يكون ذلك مستنداً إلى العلاقة، نحو: كلما كان الإنسان ناطقا فالحمار ناهق وليس كلما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الفرس ناهقا.
The connected conditional: if judgment is made of the affirmation of a relation upon another condition or its negation, it is necessary if that is with a causal relation, otherwise accidental. The negative is that in which judgment is made of the negation of their connection, like: 'Not at all whenever the sun is rising was the night existing.' The necessary affirmative is that in which judgment is made of connection with a causal relation, and the negative is that in which judgment is made that there is no connection with a causal relation, whether there is no connection or there is connection but not with a causal relation. As for the accidental, it is that in which judgment is made of mere connection or its negation, without that being based on a causal relation, like: 'Whenever a human is speaking, a donkey is braying' and 'Not whenever a human is speaking was a horse braying.'
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage defines the connected conditional proposition (shartiyya mutasila) and classifies it into two main types: necessary (luzumiyya) and accidental (ittifaqiyya). The definition states that a connected conditional is a judgment that affirms or denies a relation between two propositions on the condition of another proposition. If the connection is based on a causal relation (bī-'alāqa), it is called necessary; otherwise, it is accidental. The author then explains the affirmative and negative forms of these types. For the necessary conditional, the affirmative is when the connection is with a causal relation (e.g., 'Whenever the sun rises, day exists'), and the negative is when there is no such causal relation (e.g., 'Not whenever the sun rises, night exists'). The accidental conditional is distinguished by the absence of a causal relation; the connection is merely coincidental, as in the example 'Whenever a human speaks, a donkey brays' (there is no causal link). The passage concludes with an exhortation to reflect (fata'ammal), signaling the importance of understanding this distinction. The elaboration ensures that the student grasps that the negation of a necessary conditional can occur either when the connection is entirely absent or when it exists but without a causal relation.

Key Takeaway
The essential distinction in connected conditionals is whether the connection is causal (necessary) or coincidental (accidental).

The Continuous Conditional: Luzumiyya and Ittifaqiyya
Explains the division of continuous conditionals into necessary and accidental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a necessary (luzumiyya) and an accidental (ittifaqiyya) continuous conditional by identifying whether the conjunction is based on a relation ('ilaqa).

Core Idea
A necessary conditional has a relational glue; an accidental one is mere coincidence.

Conditional propositions in Islamic logic are not about the truth values of their parts but about the connection between them. The continuous conditional (mutassila) comes in two varieties: luzumiyya, where the connection is grounded in a relation like causation, and ittifaqiyya, where it is purely coincidental. This lesson helps you spot the difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent and consequent
In a conditional like 'Whenever the sun is rising, the day exists', find the two component propositions. Note whether they are affirmative or negative.
The relation is between the two propositions, not the terms.
2
Check for an underlying relation ('ilaqa)
Ask: Is there a reason the antecedent brings about the consequent? For example, sunrise causes day. If such a reason is present and intended, it is luzumiyya.
Relations can be causal (temporal, logical) or correlational (tadāyuf).
3
Determine if the judgment is based on the relation
Even if a relation exists in reality, the judgment may ignore it. If the judgment is made without considering the relation, the conditional is ittifaqiyya.
Example: 'Whenever man speaks, the donkey brays' — there is a correlation but no causal relation.
4
Classify as luzumiyya or ittifaqiyya
If the conjunction is asserted with a relation, it is luzumiyya. If asserted without considering any relation, it is ittifaqiyya.
The truth of the conditional does not depend on the truth of the parts.

الكذب الانتفاء لا معناهما المذكور سابقا وهو مطابقة الحكم للواقع واللامطابقة؛ لأنهما مختصان بالإخبار، وأطراف الشرطية ليس بإخبار. (برهان)
Divisions of the conditional proposition: The negative is that in which judgment is made of the negation of their conjunction, such as 'Not at all whenever the sun is rising, the night exists.' Likewise, the necessary affirmative is that in which judgment is made of conjunction with a relation, and the negative is that in which judgment is made that there is no conjunction with a relation, whether there is no conjunction or there is but not with a relation. As for the accidental, it is that in which judgment is made of mere conjunction or its negation without that being based on the relation, such as 'Whenever man is speaking, the donkey is braying' and 'Not whenever man is speaking, the horse is braying', so reflect. And the negative etc. means that affirmation and negation in the conditional are not on account of the affirmation and negation of the two parts but on account of the relationship and the judgment. For if the judgment in it is of conjunction between the two propositions, then it is affirmative; otherwise it is negative. Thus our saying 'Whenever the sun is not rising, the day does not exist' is a necessary affirmative even though the two parts are negative, and our saying 'Not at all whenever the sun is rising, the night exists' is a necessary negative even though the two parts are affirmative. (Ismail) 'by negation of their conjunction' meaning by negation of the conjunction of the two propositions whether they are affirmative, negative, or different. For example: 'Not at all whenever the sun is rising, the night exists' and 'Not at all whenever the sun is not rising, the night does not exist' and 'Not at all whenever the sun is not rising, the day exists' and 'Not at all whenever the sun is rising, the day does not exist.' 'With conjunction' meaning with conjunction of the two propositions absolutely with a relation. And analogously his saying 'there is no conjunction with a relation'; example of affirmative: 'Whenever the sun is rising, the day exists'; example of negative: 'Not whenever the sun is rising, the night exists.' 'Whether there is not etc.' because the negation of the qualified may occur by the negation of the qualified and the qualifier together, and sometimes by the negation of the qualifier only. Example of the first: 'Not at all whenever the sun is rising, the night exists'; example of the second: 'Not at all whenever man is speaking, the donkey is braying'; for although there is between man's speech and the donkey's braying an accidental conjunction, yet not with a relation, because man's speech is not a cause for the donkey's braying. 'Without that being' meaning not that it is without the relation, because the accidental is that in which judgment is made of mere conjunction and coincidence in reality without consideration of the relation, whether there is between them a relation or not. For the actual existence of the relation does not harm the truth of the accidental; the difference is that the relation is taken into account in the necessary but not in the accidental. 'So reflect' is an indication that the divisions of the conditional are three: for judgment in them is either by necessity so necessary, or by coincidence so accidental, or by absoluteness so absolute. So the third division was omitted and the answer to it is that the absolute has no realization without the necessary or the accidental, so it is included under them. (Ismail) 'With a relation, otherwise it is accidental. And the disjunctive is that in which judgment is made of the incompatibility of the two propositions or of its negation in truth and falsity together, and it is the real.' His saying 'with a relation' it is a thing by reason of which the antecedent draws the consequent, like the relation of sunrise to the existence of day in our saying 'Whenever the sun is rising, the day exists.' His saying 'of the incompatibility of the two propositions' whether the two propositions are affirmative, negative, or different. For if judgment is made of their incompatibility, then it is a disjunctive affirmative; and if judgment is made of the negation of their incompatibility, then it is a disjunctive negative. His saying 'and it is the real' so the real disjunctive is that in which judgment is made of the incompatibility of the two propositions in truth and falsity, like our saying 'Either this number is even or odd', or judgment is made of the negation of the incompatibility of the two propositions in truth and falsity, like our saying 'Not at all either this number is even or divisible into two equals.' And the disjunctive that prevents conjunction (mani'at al-jam') is that in which judgment is made of the incompatibility of the two propositions or of its absence in truth only, like 'This thing is either a tree or a stone.' And the disjunctive that prevents emptiness (mani'at al-khuluww) is that in which judgment is made of the incompatibility of the two propositions or of its absence in falsity only. Like the relation of causality of the antecedent for the consequent, or causality of the consequent for the antecedent, or both being effects of a third, such as: 'Whenever the sun is rising, the day exists', and 'Whenever the day exists, the sun is rising', and 'Whenever the day exists, the earth is illuminated'; and the relation of correlation, such as 'Whenever Zayd is the father of Amr, then Amr is his son' and 'Whenever Amr is the son of Zayd, then Zayd is his father.' 'Affirmative' like 'This number is either even or odd.' And there applies a necessary continuous conditional with affirmative antecedent and negative consequent, or vice versa, such as: 'If this number is even, then it is not odd', and 'If it is odd, then it is not even', and 'If it is not odd, then it is even', and 'If it is not even, then it is odd' — analogize to that all examples of the disjunctive conditional. Or negative such that negation is taken in both, like 'This thing is either not a tree or not a stone', then it is a disjunctive conditional in the matter of mani'at al-khuluww. And it is the real; it is called real because of the incompatibility of the two propositions and their disjunction in that they do not combine in truth and falsity. So since the incompatibility is real, the proposition is called real. 'Incompatibility of the two propositions' meaning by the impossibility that the two propositions are realized together, though they may be negated together. So the intended meaning of 'truth' is realization, and of 'falsity' is non-existence, not their previously mentioned meanings which are correspondence of judgment to reality and non-correspondence, because they are specific to reports, and the parts of the conditional are not reports. (Burhan)
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the divisions of conditional propositions (qadaya shartiyya). It begins by defining the universal (or continuous) conditional (mutassila) and its two types: lazimiyya (necessary) and ittifaqiyya (accidental). The key distinction is whether the conjunction between the antecedent and consequent is based on a relation ('ilaqa) or not. In lazimiyya, the relation is present and considered; in ittifaqiyya, the conjunction is merely coincidental without regard to any underlying connection. The commentary clarifies that affirmation and negation in conditionals concern the relation between the two propositions, not the truth of the propositions themselves. For example, 'Whenever the sun does not rise, the day does not exist' is a necessary affirmative even though both parts are negative, because the judgment affirms a necessary connection between them. The commentator then explains that the accidental conditional can still have an actual relation existing in reality, but as long as it is not taken into account in the judgment, it remains accidental. The discussion then moves to disjunctive conditionals (munfasila), which are defined by judgment of incompatibility (tanafi) between the two propositions. The real disjunctive (haqiqiyya) involves incompatibility in both truth and falsity (e.g., 'Either this number is even or odd'), meaning the two cannot both be true nor both false. Two other types are mentioned: mani'at al-jam' (preventing conjunction), which forbids both being true but allows both being false (e.g., 'This is either a tree or a stone' — it could be neither, i.e., a mineral), and mani'at al-khuluww (preventing emptiness), which forbids both being false but allows both being true (e.g., 'This thing is either not a tree or not a stone' — it cannot be both false, i.e., it must be at least one). The passage also explains various kinds of 'ilaqa: causality (of antecedent for consequent, or vice versa, or both from a third), and correlation (tadāyuf). Finally, it clarifies that in conditional propositions, 'truth' and 'falsity' refer to realization and non-existence of the relation, not to correspondence with reality as in categorical propositions.

Key Takeaway
The difference between luzumiyya and ittifaqiyya is whether the conjunction is grounded in a relation ('ilaqa) or not.

The Disjunctive Conditional: Haqiqiyya, Mani'at al-Jam', Mani'at al-Khuluw
Explains the division of disjunctive conditionals into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize any disjunctive conditional as haqiqiyya, mani'at al-jam', or mani'at al-khuluw based on the truth conditions of the disjunction.

Core Idea
Disjunctives differ by whether they forbid both true, both false, or both.

Disjunctive conditionals (munfasila) assert incompatibility between two propositions. The type of incompatibility determines the logical behavior: the real disjunctive (haqiqiyya) forbids both truth and falsity together; mani'at al-jam' forbids only conjunction; mani'at al-khuluw forbids only emptiness. This lesson equips you to identify each type from examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of the disjunctive
In a statement like 'Either this number is even or odd', the two propositions are 'number is even' and 'number is odd'.
Parts can be affirmative or negative.
2
Determine if both parts can be true simultaneously
Ask: Can both propositions be true at the same time? If not, the disjunctive prevents conjunction (mani'at al-jam'). If they can both be true, then it is not mani'at al-jam'.
Example: 'This is either a tree or a stone' — it cannot be both, so it prevents conjunction.
3
Determine if both parts can be false simultaneously
Ask: Can both propositions be false at the same time? If not, the disjunctive prevents emptiness (mani'at al-khuluw). If they can both be false, then it is not mani'at al-khuluw.
Example: 'This thing is either not a tree or not a stone' — it cannot be both false (i.e., it must be at least one), so it prevents emptiness.
4
Classify based on both checks
If both conjunction and emptiness are impossible, it is haqiqiyya (real disjunctive). If only conjunction is impossible, it is mani'at al-jam'. If only emptiness is impossible, it is mani'at al-khuluw.
The haqiqiyya is the strongest type, often used in logical proofs.

الشمس طالعة فالليل موجود ومثال الثاني: ليس البتة كلما كان الإنسان ناطقا فالحمار ناهق؛ فإنه وإن كان بين نطق الإنسان ونهق الحمار اتصال اتفاقي لكن لا بعلاقة؛ فإن نطق الإنسان ليس علة لنهق الحمار. من غير أن يكون ذلك: لا أن يكون بدون العلاقة؛ لأن الاتفاقية ما حكم فيها بمجرد الاتصال والتوافق في الواقع بدون لحاظ العلاقة سواء كان بينهما علاقة أو لا. فتحقق العلاقة في نفس الأمر لا يضر لصدق الاتفاقية، فالفرق أن العلاقة ملحوظة في اللزومية دون الاتفاقية. فتدبر إشارة إلى أن أقسام الشرطية ثلاثة؛ فإن الحكم فيهما إما باللزوم فلزومية، وإما بالاتفاق فاتفاقية أو بالإطلاق فمطلقة، فترك القسم الثالث مما وجه له والجواب عنه: أن المطلقة لا تحقق لها بدون اللزومية أو الاتفاقية فهي داخلة تحتهما. إسماعيل) لا بعلاقة، وإلا فاتفاقية ومنفصلة إن حكم فيها بتنافي النسبتين أو لا تنافيهما صدقا وكذبا معا، وهي الحقيقية • · :قوله بعلاقة وهي أمر بسببه يستصحب المقدم التالي كعلاقة طلوع الشمس لوجود النهار في :قولنا كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود قوله: بتنافي النسبتين سواء كانت النسبتان ثبوتيتين أو سلبيتين أو مختلفتين فإن كان الحكم فيها بتنافيهما فهي منفصلة موجبة، وإن كان بسلب تنافيهما فهي منفصلة سالبة قوله وهي الحقيقية فالمنفصلة الحقيقية ما حكم فيها بتنافي النسبتين في الصدق والكذب كقولنا إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجا أو فردا أو حكم فيها بسلب تنافي النسبتين في الصدق والكذب نحو قولنا: ليس البتة إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجا أو منقسما بمتساويين والمنفصلة المانعة الجمع ما حكم فيها بتنافي النسبتين أو لا تنافيها في الصدق فقط نحو هذا الشيء إما أن يكون شجرا أو حجرا والمنفصلة المانعة الخلو ما حكم فيها بتنافي النسبتين أو لا تنافيهما في الكذب فقط كعلاقة كعلاقة علية المقدم للتالي أو علية التالي للمقدم أو معلولية كليهما لثالث مثل: كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود وكلما كان النهار موجودا كانت الشمس طالعة وكلما كان النهار موجودا فالأرض مضيئة، والتضايف نحو كلما كان زيد أبا عمرو فعمرو ابنه وكلما كان عمرو ابن زيد فزيد أبوه. ثبوتيتين نحو هذا العدد إما زوج أو فرد، ويصدق هناك متصلة لزومية وجودية المقدم وسلبية التالي أو بالعكس، مثل: إن كان هذا العدد زوجا فليس بفرد وإن كان فردا فليس بزوج وإن لم يكن فردا فزوج وإن لم يكن زوجا ففرد وقس عليه سائر أمثلة الشرطية المنفصلة أو سلبيتين بأن يكون السلب مأخوذا فيهما، مثل هذا الشيء إما لا شجر وإما لا حجر فهي شرطية منفصلة في مادة مانعة الخلو. وهي الحقيقية وإنما سميت حقيقية؛ لتنافي النسبتين وانفصالهما بأن لا تجتمعا في الصدق والكذب، فلما كان التنافي فيهما على حقيقة سميت القضية حقيقية. تنافي النسبتين: أي بامتناع أن يتحقق النسبتان معا وإن تنتفيا معا، فالمراد من الصدق التحقق، ومن الكذب الانتفاء لا معناهما المذكور سابقا وهو مطابقة الحكم للواقع واللامطابقة؛ لأنهما مختصان بالإخبار، وأطراف الشرطية ليس بإخبار. (برهان)
Divisions of the conditional proposition: The negative is that in which judgment is made of the negation of their conjunction, such as 'Not at all whenever the sun is rising, the night exists.' Likewise, the necessary affirmative is that in which judgment is made of conjunction with a relation, and the negative is that in which judgment is made that there is no conjunction with a relation, whether there is no conjunction or there is but not with a relation. As for the accidental, it is that in which judgment is made of mere conjunction or its negation without that being based on the relation, such as 'Whenever man is speaking, the donkey is braying' and 'Not whenever man is speaking, the horse is braying', so reflect. And the negative etc. means that affirmation and negation in the conditional are not on account of the affirmation and negation of the two parts but on account of the relationship and the judgment. For if the judgment in it is of conjunction between the two propositions, then it is affirmative; otherwise it is negative. Thus our saying 'Whenever the sun is not rising, the day does not exist' is a necessary affirmative even though the two parts are negative, and our saying 'Not at all whenever the sun is rising, the night exists' is a necessary negative even though the two parts are affirmative. (Ismail) 'by negation of their conjunction' meaning by negation of the conjunction of the two propositions whether they are affirmative, negative, or different. For example: 'Not at all whenever the sun is rising, the night exists' and 'Not at all whenever the sun is not rising, the night does not exist' and 'Not at all whenever the sun is not rising, the day exists' and 'Not at all whenever the sun is rising, the day does not exist.' 'With conjunction' meaning with conjunction of the two propositions absolutely with a relation. And analogously his saying 'there is no conjunction with a relation'; example of affirmative: 'Whenever the sun is rising, the day exists'; example of negative: 'Not whenever the sun is rising, the night exists.' 'Whether there is not etc.' because the negation of the qualified may occur by the negation of the qualified and the qualifier together, and sometimes by the negation of the qualifier only. Example of the first: 'Not at all whenever the sun is rising, the night exists'; example of the second: 'Not at all whenever man is speaking, the donkey is braying'; for although there is between man's speech and the donkey's braying an accidental conjunction, yet not with a relation, because man's speech is not a cause for the donkey's braying. 'Without that being' meaning not that it is without the relation, because the accidental is that in which judgment is made of mere conjunction and coincidence in reality without consideration of the relation, whether there is between them a relation or not. For the actual existence of the relation does not harm the truth of the accidental; the difference is that the relation is taken into account in the necessary but not in the accidental. 'So reflect' is an indication that the divisions of the conditional are three: for judgment in them is either by necessity so necessary, or by coincidence so accidental, or by absoluteness so absolute. So the third division was omitted and the answer to it is that the absolute has no realization without the necessary or the accidental, so it is included under them. (Ismail) 'With a relation, otherwise it is accidental. And the disjunctive is that in which judgment is made of the incompatibility of the two propositions or of its negation in truth and falsity together, and it is the real.' His saying 'with a relation' it is a thing by reason of which the antecedent draws the consequent, like the relation of sunrise to the existence of day in our saying 'Whenever the sun is rising, the day exists.' His saying 'of the incompatibility of the two propositions' whether the two propositions are affirmative, negative, or different. For if judgment is made of their incompatibility, then it is a disjunctive affirmative; and if judgment is made of the negation of their incompatibility, then it is a disjunctive negative. His saying 'and it is the real' so the real disjunctive is that in which judgment is made of the incompatibility of the two propositions in truth and falsity, like our saying 'Either this number is even or odd', or judgment is made of the negation of the incompatibility of the two propositions in truth and falsity, like our saying 'Not at all either this number is even or divisible into two equals.' And the disjunctive that prevents conjunction (mani'at al-jam') is that in which judgment is made of the incompatibility of the two propositions or of its absence in truth only, like 'This thing is either a tree or a stone.' And the disjunctive that prevents emptiness (mani'at al-khuluww) is that in which judgment is made of the incompatibility of the two propositions or of its absence in falsity only. Like the relation of causality of the antecedent for the consequent, or causality of the consequent for the antecedent, or both being effects of a third, such as: 'Whenever the sun is rising, the day exists', and 'Whenever the day exists, the sun is rising', and 'Whenever the day exists, the earth is illuminated'; and the relation of correlation, such as 'Whenever Zayd is the father of Amr, then Amr is his son' and 'Whenever Amr is the son of Zayd, then Zayd is his father.' 'Affirmative' like 'This number is either even or odd.' And there applies a necessary continuous conditional with affirmative antecedent and negative consequent, or vice versa, such as: 'If this number is even, then it is not odd', and 'If it is odd, then it is not even', and 'If it is not odd, then it is even', and 'If it is not even, then it is odd' — analogize to that all examples of the disjunctive conditional. Or negative such that negation is taken in both, like 'This thing is either not a tree or not a stone', then it is a disjunctive conditional in the matter of mani'at al-khuluww. And it is the real; it is called real because of the incompatibility of the two propositions and their disjunction in that they do not combine in truth and falsity. So since the incompatibility is real, the proposition is called real. 'Incompatibility of the two propositions' meaning by the impossibility that the two propositions are realized together, though they may be negated together. So the intended meaning of 'truth' is realization, and of 'falsity' is non-existence, not their previously mentioned meanings which are correspondence of judgment to reality and non-correspondence, because they are specific to reports, and the parts of the conditional are not reports. (Burhan)
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the divisions of conditional propositions (qadaya shartiyya). It begins by defining the universal (or continuous) conditional (mutassila) and its two types: lazimiyya (necessary) and ittifaqiyya (accidental). The key distinction is whether the conjunction between the antecedent and consequent is based on a relation ('ilaqa) or not. In lazimiyya, the relation is present and considered; in ittifaqiyya, the conjunction is merely coincidental without regard to any underlying connection. The commentary clarifies that affirmation and negation in conditionals concern the relation between the two propositions, not the truth of the propositions themselves. For example, 'Whenever the sun does not rise, the day does not exist' is a necessary affirmative even though both parts are negative, because the judgment affirms a necessary connection between them. The commentator then explains that the accidental conditional can still have an actual relation existing in reality, but as long as it is not taken into account in the judgment, it remains accidental. The discussion then moves to disjunctive conditionals (munfasila), which are defined by judgment of incompatibility (tanafi) between the two propositions. The real disjunctive (haqiqiyya) involves incompatibility in both truth and falsity (e.g., 'Either this number is even or odd'), meaning the two cannot both be true nor both false. Two other types are mentioned: mani'at al-jam' (preventing conjunction), which forbids both being true but allows both being false (e.g., 'This is either a tree or a stone' — it could be neither, i.e., a mineral), and mani'at al-khuluww (preventing emptiness), which forbids both being false but allows both being true (e.g., 'This thing is either not a tree or not a stone' — it cannot be both false, i.e., it must be at least one). The passage also explains various kinds of 'ilaqa: causality (of antecedent for consequent, or vice versa, or both from a third), and correlation (tadāyuf). Finally, it clarifies that in conditional propositions, 'truth' and 'falsity' refer to realization and non-existence of the relation, not to correspondence with reality as in categorical propositions.

Checkpoint
Which type of disjunctive conditional forbids both propositions from being true together but allows both to be false?
A) Haqiqiyya (real disjunctive)
B) Mani'at al-jam' (preventing conjunction)
C) Mani'at al-khuluw (preventing emptiness)
D) Ittifaqiyya (accidental)

Key Takeaway
Disjunctive conditionals are classified by whether they prevent both true, both false, or both, with haqiqiyya being the strictest.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following disjunctive conditional: 'This act is either obligatory or forbidden.' Determine which type it is and justify your answer using the truth conditions.
?
Construct a disjunctive conditional that is mani'at al-khuluw (preventing emptiness) using an example from Islamic jurisprudence (e.g., rulings on purity).

8 min
How Conditionals Are Divided: Types and the Concept of Taqadeer
Division of conditionals into quantified, indefinite, personal, with natural excluded.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of conditional propositions (quantified universal/particular, indefinite, personal) and explain why 'natural' is not applicable, and differentiate the basis of division from categorical propositions.

Core Idea
Conditionals divide by estimations (taqadeer), not by subject individuals.

In this passage, the commentator elaborates on the division of conditional propositions, drawing a parallel to categoricals. Understanding why conditionals cannot be 'natural' and how their quantity depends on 'estimations' of the antecedent is crucial for accurate logical analysis in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parallel with categoricals
Notice that just as categoricals are divided into quantified, indefinite, personal, and natural, conditionals are divided into the first three but not natural. Why? Because conditionals involve a relation between antecedent and consequent, and their quantity is based on the number of estimations of the antecedent.
2
Understand 'taqadeer' (estimations)
Taqadeer are the various possible states or conditions under which the antecedent is considered. They are analogous to individuals of the subject in categoricals. For example, in 'Whenever the sun rises, daytime exists', the estimation is the state of the sun rising.
Taqadeer are not times but states, to include all relevant conditions.
3
Classify the four types
If the judgment of conjunction/disjunction is on a single specific estimation, it is personal (shakhsiyya). If on all estimations (universal) or some (particular), it is quantified (mahsura). If quantification is unspecified, it is indefinite (muhmala).
4
Explain why natural is excluded
Natural (tabi'iyya) is inconceivable in conditionals because a conditional always judges on estimations, not on the pure nature of the antecedent. The antecedent is always taken under some condition, not absolutely.
This is a key difference from categoricals.

ثم الحكم في الشرطية إن كان قوله ثم الحكم إلخ كما أن الحملية تنقسم إلى محصورة ومهملة وشخصية وطبيعية، كذلك الشرطية أيضاً سواء كانت متصلة أو منفصلة تنقسم إلى المحصورة الكلية والجزئية والمهملة والشخصية ولا تعقل الطبيعية ههنا.

ثم الحكم إلخ: هذا هو التقسيم الثاني للشرطية إلى المحصورة والمخصوصة والمهملة، كانقسام الحملية إليها، والفرق أن انقسام الحملية إليها باعتبار أفراد الموضوع وانقسام الشرطية إليها باعتبار تقادير المقدم أي أوضاعه ويراد بالأوضاع الأحوال العارضة للمقدم بالنظر إلى ما سواه من الأمور المقارنة للمقدم بالإمكان أو بالفعل، وإنما لم تفسر فضلا عن التقادير بالأزمنة بل بالأوضاع لاستلزام شمول الأوضاع شمول الأزمنة من غير عكس. فتدبر. (عبد الحليم) كما أن الحملية إلخ: اعلم أن تقادير الشرطيات كأفراد الحمليات؛ فإن حكم اتصالا أو انفصالا على تقدير معين فشخصية وإلا فإن بين كمية التقادير كلا أو بعضا فمحصورة كلية أو جزئية، وإلا فمهملة. ولا تعقل الطبيعية: دفع توهم عسى أن يتوهم أن المخصوصة والمحصورة كانتا من أقسام الحملية، وينقسم إليهما الشرطية، فكذا يجوز أن تنقسم مثل الحملية إلى الطبيعية أيضاً، ودفعه بأن الطبيعية في الشرطية غير معقولة أن يكون معتبرة؛ إذ الحكم في الشرطية على التقادير واعتبارها واجب فيها، فهي بمنزلة الأفراد في الحملية فيعقل بيان الكمية واهما لها ولا يعقل أخذ طبيعة المحكوم بدون التقادير، وأيضاً أن ما يحكم عليه في الشرطية لا يصلح أن يؤخذ من حيث الإطلاق والعموم فكيف تكون طبيعية وإذا لم تكن الطبيعية معقولة فكيف تكون المهملة القدمائية معقولة؛ لأن الحكم فيها على الطبيعية أيضاً مع قطع النظر عن العموم والخصوص. لا يذهب عليك أن المعدولة والمحصلة غير معقولة في الشرطية؛ إذ العدل والتحصيل لا يجريان فيها، كما يجريان في الحملية؛ لأن الاتصال والانفصال إنما يتحقق بين النسبتين في نفسهما، وهما ليستا بمعدولتين ومحصلتين باعتبار نفسهما بل باعتبار طرفيهما، فاعتبار ذلك فيهما باعتبار جزئية حرف السلب بجزء من المقدم والتالي، وإن كان ممكنا لكن لا فائدة في اعتداده وكذا الحقيقية والخارجية وإن كان اعتبارهما صحيحا باعتبار أخذ جميع التقادير الممكنة أو الاقتصار على التقادير الواقعية لكنه خارج عن حيز الاعتداد؛ لأن الحكم في الشرطية ليس بمقصور على التقادير الواقعية بل شامل لجميع التقادير من مرآة الشروح باختصار

على جميع تقادير المقدم فكلية أو بعضها مطلقاً فجزئية، أو معيناً فشخصية،

قوله: على جميع تقادير المقدم :كقولنا كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود. قوله: فكلية وسورها في المتصلة الموجبة "كلما" و"مهما" و"متى" وما في معناها، وفي المنفصلة و "أبدا" ونحوهما، هذا في الموجبة. أما في السالبة مطلقا فسورها "ليس البتة". قوله: أو بعضها مطلقا أي على بعض غير معين كقولك: قد يكون إذا كان الشيء حيوانا كان إنسانا قوله فجزئية وسورها في الموجبة متصلة كانت أو منفصلة قد يكون" وفي السالبة كذلك "قد لا يكون": قوله فشخصية

تقادير المقدم والتقادير هي الأحوال والأوضاع واقعية كانت أو فرضية ممكنة الاجتماع مع المقدم بحيث لا يصادم اللزوم والعناد، وإنما اعتبر في الأوضاع أن تكون ممكنة الاجتماع؛ لأنه لو اعتبر جميع الأوضاع مطلقا سواء كانت ممكنة الاجتماع أو لا تكون لم يصدق شرطية كلية؛ إما في الاتصال فلأن من الأوضاع ما لا يلزم معه التالي كعدم التالي أو عدم لزوم التالي فلا يكون التالي لازما له على هذا الوضع وإلا لكان المقدم على هذا الوضع مستلزماً للنقيضين وإنه محال. فعلى بعض الأوضاع لا يكون التالي لازما للمقدم، فلا يصدق أن التالي لازم المقدم على جميع الأوضاع، وهو مفهوم الكلية على ذلك التقدير، وأما في الانفصال فلأن من الأوضاع ما لا يعاند التالي للمقدم معه كصدق الطرفين؛ فإن التالي على هذا الوضع لازم للمقدم فيكون نقيض التالي معاندا للمقدم، فلو كان المقدم معاندا للتالي على هذا الوضع لزم معاندة الشيء للنقيضين وإنه محال، فعلى بعض الأوضاع لا يعاند التالي للمقدم فلا يصدق أن التالي معاند للمقدم على سائر الأوضاع. (تحرير القواعد المنطقية) وسورها في المتصلة الموجبة: اعلم أن سور المتصلة الموجبة الكلية "كلما" و"متى" و"مهما" وما في معناها بأي لغة كانت وللمنفصلة كذلك "دائماً" و"أبدا" ونحوهما، ولسالبتهما "ليس "البتة" وللإيجاب والسلب الجزئيين فيهما "قد يكون" و"قد لا يكون وللمتصلة وحدها ليس كلما وللمنفصلة وحدها "ليس دائما" وأداة المهملات المتصلة "إن" و"لو" و"إذا" وللمنفصلة "إما" و"أو " والشرطية مطلقة إن لم يذكر فيها الجهة وموجهة إن ذكرت جهة اللزوم أو العناد أو الاتفاق كقولك: بالضرورة كلما كان "" فـ"ج" ولزوما أو اتفاقا وبالضرورة دائما إما أن يكون "" "ب" "أو " "ج" وعنادا أو اتفاقا.

إذا كان الشيء إلخ : فإن الحكم فيها بلزوم الإنسانية إنما هو على بعض تقادير كونه حيوانا.(شيخ الإسلام)
Then the judgment in the conditional if, his saying then the judgment etc.: Just as the categorical is divided into quantified, indefinite, personal, and natural, similarly the conditional, whether conjunctive or disjunctive, is divided into quantified universal and particular, indefinite, and personal, and the natural is not conceivable here.

Then the judgment etc.: This is the second division of the conditional into quantified, particularized, and indefinite, like the division of the categorical into them, and the difference is that the division of the categorical into them is by consideration of the individuals of the subject, while the division of the conditional into them is by consideration of the estimations of the antecedent, i.e., its states. By 'states' are meant the accidental conditions of the antecedent with respect to what is other than it of matters concomitant with the antecedent potentially or actually. And it was not interpreted as 'times' but as 'states' because the inclusiveness of states entails the inclusiveness of times without the converse. So reflect. (Abd al-Halim) As the categorical etc.: Know that the estimations of conditionals are like the individuals of categoricals; for if the judgment of conjunction or disjunction is on a specific estimation, it is personal; otherwise, if the quantity of estimations is all or some, it is quantified universal or particular; otherwise, it is indefinite. And the natural is not conceivable: a rebuttal of the supposition that perhaps one might suppose that the particularized and quantified were divisions of the categorical, and the conditional is divided into them, so likewise it may be divided into the natural as well, like the categorical. Its rebuttal is that the natural in the conditional is not conceivable as being considered, because the judgment in the conditional is on the estimations, and their consideration is obligatory in it, so they are like the individuals in the categorical, so the statement of quantity is conceivable in the mind for them, but taking the nature of the judged without the estimations is not conceivable. Also, what is judged in the conditional cannot be taken from the aspect of absoluteness and generality, so how can it be natural? And if the natural is not conceivable, then how can the pre-modern indefinite be conceivable? Because the judgment in it is on the nature as well, regardless of generality and particularity. Do not forget that the modified and the simple are not conceivable in the conditional, because modification and simplicity do not occur in it as they occur in the categorical; because conjunction and disjunction occur only between the two relations themselves, and they are not modified or simple in themselves but by consideration of their extremes. So considering that in them by considering the negative particle as part of the antecedent or consequent, although possible, has no benefit. Similarly, the real and external, although their consideration is correct by taking all possible estimations or restricting to actual estimations, it is outside the scope of consideration, because the judgment in the conditional is not confined to actual estimations but includes all estimations. From Mir'at al-Shurh bi-ikhtisar.

On all estimations of the antecedent, it is universal; or some absolutely, it is particular; or a specific [estimation], it is personal.

His saying: On all estimations of the antecedent: like our saying: Whenever the sun rises, daytime exists. His saying: It is universal, and its quantifier in the affirmative conjunctive is 'kullama', 'mahma', 'mata', and what is similar; in the disjunctive it is 'abadan' and the like. This is in the affirmative. As for the negative universally, its quantifier is 'laysa al-battata'. His saying: Or some absolutely, i.e., on some non-specific estimation, like your saying: It may be that if something is an animal, it is a human. His saying: It is particular, and its quantifier in the affirmative, whether conjunctive or disjunctive, is 'qad yakunu' and in the negative similarly 'qad la yakunu'. His saying: So it is personal.

The estimations of the antecedent: The estimations are the states and conditions, whether actual or hypothetical, that can be combined with the antecedent such that they do not conflict with the implication or opposition. And the requirement that the states be possible to be combined is considered because if all states absolutely were considered whether possible to be combined or not, a universal conditional would not be true; in the conjunctive, because there are states in which the consequent is not implied, like non-existence of the consequent or non-implication of the consequent, so the consequent would not be implied for it in that state, otherwise the antecedent in that state would imply two contradictories, which is impossible. So in some states the consequent is not implied by the antecedent, so it is not true that the consequent is implied by the antecedent on all states, which is the concept of universality on that estimation. As for the disjunctive, because there are states in which the consequent is not opposed to the antecedent with it, like the truth of both extremes; for the consequent in this state is implied by the antecedent, so the negation of the consequent is opposed to the antecedent; so if the antecedent were opposed to the consequent in this state, then a thing would be opposed to two contradictories, which is impossible. So in some states the consequent is not opposed to the antecedent, so it is not true that the consequent is opposed to the antecedent on all states. (Tahrir al-Qawa'id al-Mantiqiyya) And their quantifiers in the affirmative conjunctive: Know that the quantifiers of the universal affirmative conjunctive are 'kullama', 'mata', 'mahma', and what means the same in any language; for the disjunctive similarly are 'da'iman', 'abadan', and the like; for their negative are 'laysa al-battata'; for the particular affirmative and negative in both are 'qad yakunu' and 'qad la yakunu'; for the conjunctive alone is 'laysa kullama'; for the disjunctive alone is 'laysa da'iman'; the particles for indefinite conjunctives are 'in', 'law', 'idha'; and for disjunctives are 'imma' and 'aw'. A conditional is absolute if its modality is not mentioned, and modal if the modality of implication or opposition or agreement is mentioned, like saying: Necessarily, whenever it is ... then ... for necessity or agreement; and necessarily always either ... or ... for opposition or agreement.

If something etc.: For the judgment in it of the necessity of humanity is only on some estimations of its being an animal. (Shaykh al-Islam)
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Tahzeeb explains the division of conditional propositions (al-qadiyya al-shartiyya) and their quantifiers. It begins by stating that conditionals, like categoricals, are divided into four types: quantified universal (mahsura kulliyya), quantified particular (juz'iyya), indefinite (muhmala), and personal (shakhsiyya). Unlike categoricals, where the division is based on individuals of the subject, in conditionals the division is based on 'estimations' (taqadeer) of the antecedent, meaning the various states or conditions under which the antecedent is considered. The commentator emphasizes that 'natural' (tabi'iyya) conditionals are not conceivable because a conditional always involves a judgment on a specific estimation, not on a pure nature.

The passage then lists the quantifier words (sawar) for each type. For universal affirmative conjunctive conditionals, the quantifiers are 'kullama', 'mahma', 'mata', etc.; for universal negative, 'laysa al-battata'. For particular affirmatives (both conjunctive and disjunctive), the quantifier is 'qad yakunu'; for negatives, 'qad la yakunu'. For indefinite conjunctives, the particles are 'in', 'law', 'idha'; for disjunctives, 'imma' and 'aw'.

The commentator also discusses a crucial condition for the truth of universal conditionals: the estimations must be such that they can coexist with the antecedent without contradicting the implication (in conjunctive) or opposition (in disjunctive). If all estimations, including impossible ones, were considered, the universal conditional would fail. For conjunctives, some estimations might make the consequent not follow; for disjunctives, some estimations might not make the consequent opposite. Thus, the quantifier 'kullama' applies only to estimations that are 'possible of combination' with the antecedent.

Finally, the text mentions modal conditionals, where a modality like 'bil-darura' (necessarily) is specified, indicating necessity, agreement, or opposition.

Key Takeaway
Conditional propositions are divided by the quantity of estimations (taqadeer) of the antecedent, not by subject individuals, and natural conditionals are not conceivable.

Propositions
beginner
8 min
Quantifier Words for Conditional Propositions (Sawar)
Listing of quantifier words for types of conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to recall and match the correct Arabic quantifier words (e.g., kullama, qad yakunu, etc.) to each type of conditional (affirmative/negative, universal/particular, conjunctive/disjunctive).

Core Idea
Each conditional type has its own quantifier word; knowing them is key to analyzing conditional arguments.

After understanding the division of conditionals, the commentator lists the specific quantifier words (sawar) that indicate the quantity and quality of the conditional. Mastering these quantifiers is essential for correctly interpreting and constructing conditional statements in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Learn universal affirmative quantifiers
For conjunctive conditionals (mutassila), universal affirmative uses 'kullama', 'mahma', 'mata'. Example: 'Kullama kanat al-shamsu tali'atan fa al-naharu mawjud' (Whenever the sun rises, day exists). For disjunctive (munfasila), use 'abadan' or 'da'iman'.
2
Learn universal negative quantifier
For both conjunctive and disjunctive, universal negative uses 'laysa al-battata' (not at all).
3
Learn particular quantifiers
For particular affirmative, use 'qad yakunu' (it may be) for both conjunctive and disjunctive. For particular negative, use 'qad la yakunu' (it may not be).
4
Learn indefinite particles
Indefinite conjunctives use the particles 'in', 'law', 'idha' (if, when). Indefinite disjunctives use 'imma' (either) or 'aw' (or).
5
Recognize modality indicators
When a conditional is modal (muwajjaha), it includes a modality word like 'bil-darura' (necessarily) before the quantifier, indicating necessity, agreement (ittifaq), or opposition ('inad).

ثم الحكم في الشرطية إن كان قوله ثم الحكم إلخ كما أن الحملية تنقسم إلى محصورة ومهملة وشخصية وطبيعية، كذلك الشرطية أيضاً سواء كانت متصلة أو منفصلة تنقسم إلى المحصورة الكلية والجزئية والمهملة والشخصية ولا تعقل الطبيعية ههنا.

ثم الحكم إلخ: هذا هو التقسيم الثاني للشرطية إلى المحصورة والمخصوصة والمهملة، كانقسام الحملية إليها، والفرق أن انقسام الحملية إليها باعتبار أفراد الموضوع وانقسام الشرطية إليها باعتبار تقادير المقدم أي أوضاعه ويراد بالأوضاع الأحوال العارضة للمقدم بالنظر إلى ما سواه من الأمور المقارنة للمقدم بالإمكان أو بالفعل، وإنما لم تفسر فضلا عن التقادير بالأزمنة بل بالأوضاع لاستلزام شمول الأوضاع شمول الأزمنة من غير عكس. فتدبر. (عبد الحليم) كما أن الحملية إلخ: اعلم أن تقادير الشرطيات كأفراد الحمليات؛ فإن حكم اتصالا أو انفصالا على تقدير معين فشخصية وإلا فإن بين كمية التقادير كلا أو بعضا فمحصورة كلية أو جزئية، وإلا فمهملة. ولا تعقل الطبيعية: دفع توهم عسى أن يتوهم أن المخصوصة والمحصورة كانتا من أقسام الحملية، وينقسم إليهما الشرطية، فكذا يجوز أن تنقسم مثل الحملية إلى الطبيعية أيضاً، ودفعه بأن الطبيعية في الشرطية غير معقولة أن يكون معتبرة؛ إذ الحكم في الشرطية على التقادير واعتبارها واجب فيها، فهي بمنزلة الأفراد في الحملية فيعقل بيان الكمية واهما لها ولا يعقل أخذ طبيعة المحكوم بدون التقادير، وأيضاً أن ما يحكم عليه في الشرطية لا يصلح أن يؤخذ من حيث الإطلاق والعموم فكيف تكون طبيعية وإذا لم تكن الطبيعية معقولة فكيف تكون المهملة القدمائية معقولة؛ لأن الحكم فيها على الطبيعية أيضاً مع قطع النظر عن العموم والخصوص. لا يذهب عليك أن المعدولة والمحصلة غير معقولة في الشرطية؛ إذ العدل والتحصيل لا يجريان فيها، كما يجريان في الحملية؛ لأن الاتصال والانفصال إنما يتحقق بين النسبتين في نفسهما، وهما ليستا بمعدولتين ومحصلتين باعتبار نفسهما بل باعتبار طرفيهما، فاعتبار ذلك فيهما باعتبار جزئية حرف السلب بجزء من المقدم والتالي، وإن كان ممكنا لكن لا فائدة في اعتداده وكذا الحقيقية والخارجية وإن كان اعتبارهما صحيحا باعتبار أخذ جميع التقادير الممكنة أو الاقتصار على التقادير الواقعية لكنه خارج عن حيز الاعتداد؛ لأن الحكم في الشرطية ليس بمقصور على التقادير الواقعية بل شامل لجميع التقادير من مرآة الشروح باختصار

على جميع تقادير المقدم فكلية أو بعضها مطلقاً فجزئية، أو معيناً فشخصية،

قوله: على جميع تقادير المقدم :كقولنا كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود. قوله: فكلية وسورها في المتصلة الموجبة "كلما" و"مهما" و"متى" وما في معناها، وفي المنفصلة و "أبدا" ونحوهما، هذا في الموجبة. أما في السالبة مطلقا فسورها "ليس البتة". قوله: أو بعضها مطلقا أي على بعض غير معين كقولك: قد يكون إذا كان الشيء حيوانا كان إنسانا قوله فجزئية وسورها في الموجبة متصلة كانت أو منفصلة قد يكون" وفي السالبة كذلك "قد لا يكون": قوله فشخصية

تقادير المقدم والتقادير هي الأحوال والأوضاع واقعية كانت أو فرضية ممكنة الاجتماع مع المقدم بحيث لا يصادم اللزوم والعناد، وإنما اعتبر في الأوضاع أن تكون ممكنة الاجتماع؛ لأنه لو اعتبر جميع الأوضاع مطلقا سواء كانت ممكنة الاجتماع أو لا تكون لم يصدق شرطية كلية؛ إما في الاتصال فلأن من الأوضاع ما لا يلزم معه التالي كعدم التالي أو عدم لزوم التالي فلا يكون التالي لازما له على هذا الوضع وإلا لكان المقدم على هذا الوضع مستلزماً للنقيضين وإنه محال. فعلى بعض الأوضاع لا يكون التالي لازما للمقدم، فلا يصدق أن التالي لازم المقدم على جميع الأوضاع، وهو مفهوم الكلية على ذلك التقدير، وأما في الانفصال فلأن من الأوضاع ما لا يعاند التالي للمقدم معه كصدق الطرفين؛ فإن التالي على هذا الوضع لازم للمقدم فيكون نقيض التالي معاندا للمقدم، فلو كان المقدم معاندا للتالي على هذا الوضع لزم معاندة الشيء للنقيضين وإنه محال، فعلى بعض الأوضاع لا يعاند التالي للمقدم فلا يصدق أن التالي معاند للمقدم على سائر الأوضاع. (تحرير القواعد المنطقية) وسورها في المتصلة الموجبة: اعلم أن سور المتصلة الموجبة الكلية "كلما" و"متى" و"مهما" وما في معناها بأي لغة كانت وللمنفصلة كذلك "دائماً" و"أبدا" ونحوهما، ولسالبتهما "ليس "البتة" وللإيجاب والسلب الجزئيين فيهما "قد يكون" و"قد لا يكون وللمتصلة وحدها ليس كلما وللمنفصلة وحدها "ليس دائما" وأداة المهملات المتصلة "إن" و"لو" و"إذا" وللمنفصلة "إما" و"أو " والشرطية مطلقة إن لم يذكر فيها الجهة وموجهة إن ذكرت جهة اللزوم أو العناد أو الاتفاق كقولك: بالضرورة كلما كان "" فـ"ج" ولزوما أو اتفاقا وبالضرورة دائما إما أن يكون "" "ب" "أو " "ج" وعنادا أو اتفاقا.

إذا كان الشيء إلخ : فإن الحكم فيها بلزوم الإنسانية إنما هو على بعض تقادير كونه حيوانا.(شيخ الإسلام)
Then the judgment in the conditional if, his saying then the judgment etc.: Just as the categorical is divided into quantified, indefinite, personal, and natural, similarly the conditional, whether conjunctive or disjunctive, is divided into quantified universal and particular, indefinite, and personal, and the natural is not conceivable here.

Then the judgment etc.: This is the second division of the conditional into quantified, particularized, and indefinite, like the division of the categorical into them, and the difference is that the division of the categorical into them is by consideration of the individuals of the subject, while the division of the conditional into them is by consideration of the estimations of the antecedent, i.e., its states. By 'states' are meant the accidental conditions of the antecedent with respect to what is other than it of matters concomitant with the antecedent potentially or actually. And it was not interpreted as 'times' but as 'states' because the inclusiveness of states entails the inclusiveness of times without the converse. So reflect. (Abd al-Halim) As the categorical etc.: Know that the estimations of conditionals are like the individuals of categoricals; for if the judgment of conjunction or disjunction is on a specific estimation, it is personal; otherwise, if the quantity of estimations is all or some, it is quantified universal or particular; otherwise, it is indefinite. And the natural is not conceivable: a rebuttal of the supposition that perhaps one might suppose that the particularized and quantified were divisions of the categorical, and the conditional is divided into them, so likewise it may be divided into the natural as well, like the categorical. Its rebuttal is that the natural in the conditional is not conceivable as being considered, because the judgment in the conditional is on the estimations, and their consideration is obligatory in it, so they are like the individuals in the categorical, so the statement of quantity is conceivable in the mind for them, but taking the nature of the judged without the estimations is not conceivable. Also, what is judged in the conditional cannot be taken from the aspect of absoluteness and generality, so how can it be natural? And if the natural is not conceivable, then how can the pre-modern indefinite be conceivable? Because the judgment in it is on the nature as well, regardless of generality and particularity. Do not forget that the modified and the simple are not conceivable in the conditional, because modification and simplicity do not occur in it as they occur in the categorical; because conjunction and disjunction occur only between the two relations themselves, and they are not modified or simple in themselves but by consideration of their extremes. So considering that in them by considering the negative particle as part of the antecedent or consequent, although possible, has no benefit. Similarly, the real and external, although their consideration is correct by taking all possible estimations or restricting to actual estimations, it is outside the scope of consideration, because the judgment in the conditional is not confined to actual estimations but includes all estimations. Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Tahzeeb explains the division of conditional propositions (al-qadiyya al-shartiyya) and their quantifiers. It begins by stating that conditionals, like categoricals, are divided into four types: quantified universal (mahsura kulliyya), quantified particular (juz'iyya), indefinite (muhmala), and personal (shakhsiyya). Unlike categoricals, where the division is based on individuals of the subject, in conditionals the division is based on 'estimations' (taqadeer) of the antecedent, meaning the various states or conditions under which the antecedent is considered. The commentator emphasizes that 'natural' (tabi'iyya) conditionals are not conceivable because a conditional always involves a judgment on a specific estimation, not on a pure nature.

The passage then lists the quantifier words (sawar) for each type. For universal affirmative conjunctive conditionals, the quantifiers are 'kullama', 'mahma', 'mata', etc.; for universal negative, 'laysa al-battata'. For particular affirmatives (both conjunctive and disjunctive), the quantifier is 'qad yakunu'; for negatives, 'qad la yakunu'. For indefinite conjunctives, the particles are 'in', 'law', 'idha'; for disjunctives, 'imma' and 'aw'.

The commentator also discusses a crucial condition for the truth of universal conditionals: the estimations must be such that they can coexist with the antecedent without contradicting the implication (in conjunctive) or opposition (in disjunctive). If all estimations, including impossible ones, were considered, the universal conditional would fail. For conjunctives, some estimations might make the consequent not follow; for disjunctives, some estimations might not make the consequent opposite. Thus, the quantifier 'kullama' applies only to estimations that are 'possible of combination' with the antecedent.

Finally, the text mentions modal conditionals, where a modality like 'bil-darura' (necessarily) is specified, indicating necessity, agreement, or opposition.
